General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Edward A L Turner ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥


♠ UTM zone ♣ S 38 ♥

Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 488-642 CE ♥

_Sasanid Period 1_ 205-48 CE

Conquest from 205 CE

"The Sasanian campaign to control the province of Persis/Fars had begun in 205-6, when the father of Ardashir I, Pabag, had dethroned the local ruler of the city of Istakhr, the capital of Fars, by the name of Gozihr."[1] Later sources claimed Pabag was a priest at a fire-temple in Istakhr.[2]
"King Papak, who usurped the crown of the Pars rulers, played a major role in unifying the land. He apparently had to wage a difficult struggle against the central Parthian government."[3]

Empire from 226 CE (with God-king and Achaemenid ideology)

King Papak's adopted son Ardashir inherited the crown. He was from the family of Sasan.[4]
The first Sasanian 'King of Kings' was Ardashir I who was crowned in 226 CE at Ctesiphon.[5]
Early Sasanids in their imperial ideology "considered themselves from the lineage of the gods" and used the Achaemenid title "King of Kings."[6]
Size of court and bureaucracy increases between Ardashir I and Shapur I (240-270 CE). Military success under Shapur I (240-270 CE) and Shapur II (309-379 CE). [7]

Rise of Zoroastrian Church under Kerdir 274 CE

Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[8]
Zoroastrian priest Kerdir "began the persecution of the religious minorities in the empire, such as the Jews, Christians, Manichaeans, Mandeans, and Buddhists. ... Mani ... was imprisoned and put to death in 276 with the blessing (and to the relief) of Kerdir."[9]

Status quo from 294-325 CE (Zoroastrian control)

presumably the situation is the same under kings Narseh (293-303 CE) and Hormizd II (303-309 CE) and during the infancy of Shapur II when "the court and the Zoroastrian priests ran an empire that was secure and stable enough structurally and administratively to survive without a strong monarchy"[10]

Long reign of Shapur II and rise of court/bureaucracy

Under Shapur II, power of the nobility and priests increased substantially.[11] Does this imply at some point following the church of Kerdir and his persecutions the influence of priests diminished - perhaps due to the rise of the bureaucracy/court which may have accellerated during the infancy of Shapur II?
Time of Shapur II has been referred to as a golden age.

Violence begins from 379 CE

An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[12] The sophisticated, centralised bureaucracy was now "under the control of the priests" and its chief priest, with Kingship relegated to the status of a secular institution.[13] Ardashar may have purged a court/bureaucracy which had become over-mighty during the long (70-year) reign of Shapur II. This would have favoured the Zoroastrian priests.
The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[14] that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). this elite conflict reflects a power-struggle between the court/bureaucracy and the Zoroastrian church.

recognition of Nestorian Christianity 410 CE; ends with usurper 420 CE

Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE) called "the sinful one"[15] by Zoroastrian literature because he went against the wishes of the Zoroastrian priests.
the 'secular' kings become powerful enough to challenge the priests. Under Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE) Christianity was officially recognized.[16]
"first synod of the Nestorian Church was convened in 410" during reign of Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE).[17] "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[18]

Persecution of Christians and Jews from c.420 CE

Bahram V (420-438 CE) and Yazdgird II (438-457 CE) persecuted Christians
"Bahram V continued and intensified the persecution of Yazdagird's last days." Forced conversions. Property confiscated. Churches destroyed. [19]
Yazdgird II (438-457 CE) is noted for his persecution of Christians and Jews.

Infighting from 457 CE, famine and Hephthalites

Hormizd III (457-459 CE) defeated in battle by Peroz (459-484 CE) who was aided by Hephthalites (?)
Seven-year famine (464-471)
War with Kidarites and Hephthalites
Peroz captured by Hephthalites
Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[20]
The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[21]


_Sasanid Period 2_ 488-642 CE

Reforms during the long reigns of Kavad I and Khusrau I

Kavad I (499-531 CE) 21. Khusrau I (531-579 CE)
Khusrau I (531-579 CE) promoted minor nobility and reduced the power of aristocrats and their estates. Deghans became tax collectors. "For the first time, the power of the landed nobility was restricted and all the taxes were in the hands of the king."[22]
Khusrau I is credited with wise leadership and is known as "Plato's philosopher king."[23] In 570s CE Sasanian Empire was "at the apex of its glory and power, headed by a philosopher king" (Khosrau I).

Instablity from 579 CE

Hamizid IV (579-590 CE), who followed Khosrau I, had many enemies at court, killed many of the nobility and was harsh to the priests.
Hormizd IV deposed 589-590 CE by general and nobility who put on the throne his son, Khusrau II.[24]
Khusrau II forced to flee to Byzantium for the years 590-591 CE by Bahram but recruited an Armenenian army to regain the throne.[25]
Kushrau II was deposed by nobility and priests in 628 CE.[26] Khosrau II (590-628 CE) was forced to seek shelter in Byzantine Hierapolis against a challenger king, Wahram Chubin, who minted coins 590-591 CE. Khosrau II regained the throne (purges?) and then the empire reached its greatest territorial extent. Khosrau II was deposed by priests and nobility in 628 CE.
Kavad II (628-630 CE) conducted a fratricide, killing all the male heirs in the Sasanid family, and was assassinated.[27]
By 630s CE the empire was in confusion, had disintegrated into regional power-bases and internal conflict when Khuzistan fell to Caliph Umar. Arabs conquered the Sasanid stronghold (Persis) in 650 CE.


♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state ♥

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ alliance ♥

Alliance

Ruler of South Arabia 527 CE: "This Arab king of Jewish faith, who ruled a territory corresponding approximately with the modern state of the Yemen, closed the caravan route which led to Gaza in Palestine. This affected the great trade route from the ports on the Indian Ocean via Mecca and Medina to the Mediterranean port of Gaza. It was used by much of the trade in incense and spices. And as if that were not sufficient this South Arabian ruler, who had close political alignments with Persia, had the narrow navigable channel leading through the route from Bab al-Mandab closed with the aid of a chain. Obviously he was helped in this by Persian technicians."[28]

Map[edit]

General Description[edit]

In the final Sassanid period (488-642 CE), best known for the reign of Khusrau I, the Sassanid realm was managed through provincial governors called Shahr[32] and vassal kings appointed by the Sassanid King of Kings. Its population peaked at about 22 million at around 600 CE a time when the Empire was disintegrating.

The long reigns of Kavad I (499-531 CE) and Khusrau I (531-579 CE) saw many reforms, such as to the tax system and the military. Khusrau I is credited with wise leadership and is known as "Plato's philosopher king."[33] Khusrau I (531-579 CE) promoted minor nobility to official positions and reduced the power of aristocrats, placing tax collection in the hands of officials directly under his own control.[34] Khusrau I also made some important structural reforms to the military which removed the Commander-in-Chief (Eran-Spahbad) and divided the empire into four regions, each under the control of a regional field marshal (Spahbads).[35] This change enabled the Sassanids to more quickly respond to the external threats of invasion.

After the high point of Khusrau I internal instability became the norm and the ruling dynasty had almost wiped itself out by the time the Arabs conquered the last Sassanid stronghold at Persis in 650 CE. Hamizid IV (579-590 CE), who followed Khosrau I killed many of the nobility and was harsh to the priests. Hormizd IV was deposed 589-590 CE by his generals and the nobility who put on the throne his son, Khusrau II.[36] Kushrau II was himself deposed by nobility and priests in 628 CE.[37] Kavad II (628-630 CE) conducted a fratricide, killing all the male heirs in the Sasanid family, and was assassinated.[38]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ 6 ♥


1. King of Kings[46]

_Central government_

2. Grand Vizier
Administration based in Ctesiphon
"Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[47]
3. Secretaries of a divan (ministry)[48]
4. Scribe in central administration inferred
5. Manager of state-run granary inferred, silk workshops
"As with the Parthians, the economy was based on agriculture."[49]
"State monopolies rivalled private concerns; in particular, raw silk from China was woven at workshops in Susa, Gundeshapur and Shustar."[50]
6. Worker in state-run granary inferred or silk workshop


_Provincial government_

2. Shahrabs
Governors of a province called Shahr[51]
2. King, appointed by King of Kings[52]
Kings of a district called shahr[53]
3. shahrab and mowbed
Government run by a shahrab and a mowbed and often an accountant.[54]
Mowbed had responsibility for property and legal matters.[55]
4. lesser administrators
4. Official of a division called rustag (number of villages)[56]
This administrator reported to a local government official?
5. Deghan of a division called deh (village)[57]

Unlike the religious and military institutions, the administrative system did not undergo a "quadpartite" reform at the start of this period.


♠ Religious levels ♣ [4-5] ♥

_Zoroastrianism_ "or more exactly Mazdaism"[58]

Third-century CE Zoroastrian two priests were highly influential in the development of Zoroastrianism as the Sasanid state religion (three if we include Pabag, the founder of the Sasanian dynasty, who was a priest).

Kerdir "may be considered the father of the Zoroastrian church in this period, as he was the one who attempted to make Zoroastrianism into a uniform body, with a unified doctrine, attached to the state."[59]
"The Sasanian sources state that Tosar was responsible for the codification of the Avesta ... Kerdir brought about the organization of the church and a religious hierarchy."[60]


1. King of Kings (until Shapur II)

2. Ohrmaz mowbed (chief priest)[61] mowbedan mowbed[62]
mowbedan mowbed was the "head of the religious order"[63]
"When Sassanid kings were raised to the throne they received the insignia of royal authority from the chief Mobedh who held the highest religious office."[64]
3. mowbed (district level) (head priest)[65]
"important functions and carried out legal as well as religious and administrative duties.[66]
4. mow/mog
The magus (mow/mog) had a higher status and later was also involved in economic and legal matters. Above him was the chief magus (mowbed), who held an important position and was probably the main religious authority throughout the empire."[67]
4-5. herbeds (teacher priests)[68]
"instructed the people in daily ritual, prayer, and tradition and tended the fire."
"Three major fire-temples were established for the three classes ... Smaller fire-temples existed in the villages and towns, attended by a teacher-priest (herbed)."[69]

"Magians had a hierarchy parallel to that of the state, a hierarchical judicial administration specifically for Zoroastrians, a cult, scriptures, religious laws, and distinctive customs. It was the religion of the elite and rulers."[70]

Eight different priests required for some Zoroastrian rituals e.g. vispered ritual and the videvad sade purification ritual "who took up specific positions in the ritual area, also described in the Nirangestan."[71]

haoma-pressing priest (hawanan)
fire-lighting priest (atr-wakhsh)
presenting priest (frabertar)
tending priest, who brings water (abert or danu-uzwaza, which refers to the river Danu)
washing priest (asnatar)
mingling priest (raethwish-kar)
auditing priest (sraoshawarz)
one who brings sacrificial animal (pasu-wazah)

Comprehensive source on Zoroastrian religion: Moazami (2016) "Zoroastrianism: Religious texts, theology, history and culture."[72]

_Nestorian Christianity_

"first synod of the Nestorian Church was convened in 410" during reign of Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE).[73] "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[74] "The Sasanian state used the churches as intermediaries to regulate and tax the population."[75]


1. Patriach

"Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon".[76]
the Sasanid king "organized a Christian Persian church that grew in number, and many in the royal family and the nobility, especially the women, gravitated toward this religion."[77]
2. Catholicos in province
"The Christian community was headed by the Catholicos"[78]
"The Catholicos in each province oversaw the Christian congregation and provided money and guidance for the community."[79]
3. Metropolitan
"The Sasanians appointed a catholicos or patriarch and a metropolitan to preside over the bishops in parallel with the Sasanian administrative hierarchy." [80]
4. Bishops of Bishoprics
"According to al-Biruni, Christianity had reached Merv within 200 years of the birth of Christ and the first reference to a Merv bishopric dates to the year 334."[81]
5. Heads of Churches
"by the end of the Sasanian period there were churches and bishoprics established throughout the empire, and many from the royal family also converted to Christianity."[82]
"Royal permission was required for the election of the heads of churches, for construction of buildings, for burials, and even for the issue of monastic rules."[83]
6.

From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline." [84]

_Buddhism_

"The Buddhas of Bamiyan and a number of Iranian texts in the Sogdian and Khotanese languages are testaments to the importance of Buddhism in eastern Iran."[87]


_Manicheanism_

"Manicheans moved east and westward, through some still remained in Iran, to write down their tradition and spread it among all people."[88]

Manichaean community in Merv mid-3rd CE.[89]

♠ Military levels ♣ [7-9] ♥ Like the Achaemenids, the Sasanids likely used the decimal system to organize the Spah (army). The title Hazarmard/Hazarbad means "chief of a thousand."

The reforms of Khosrau I removed the Commander-in-Chief (Eran-Spahbad) and divided the empire into four regions, each under the control of a regional field marshal (Spahbads).[90] Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) changed the command structure. "Previously the entire army had been under the command of an officer known as the spahbad. Now, four commanders were appointed, each in charge of the troops of one-quarter of the country. Each of these newly created commanders had a deputy called a marzban."[91]

1. King

2. Royal Guard (1,000, commanded by a Pushtighban-Salar)
2. Great commander (Vuzurg-Framander. Managed state affairs whilst monarch on military expedition).
2. Four regional Spahbads (field marshals)
3. Marzban
4. Gund (large regular division, lead by Gund-Salar, a general) - Marzban is another term for a general who took orders from a Spahbad
5. Immortals (10,000, commanded by a Varthragh-Nighan Khuadhay)
6. Drafsh (known to be a unit of 1,000 soldiers) - Is this the level of the Framandar, battlefield commander?
7. Vasht (small company)
(8. Unit of 10 soldiers?)
9. Individual soldier

Other units:[92]

Saravan (Commanded by an Aspbad and a Sadar)

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥

♠ Professional military officers ♣ absent: 488-531 CE; present: 531-642 CE ♥

Before the reforms of Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) "all nobles, great and small, had been obliged to equip themselves and their followers and serve in the army without pay, but Khusrau issued equipment to the poorer nobles and paid a salary for their services. Consequently, the power of the great nobles - who frequently had their own private armies - was reduced."[93]

Central registry for military created by Khosrau I (531-579 CE) [94], who also began to pay Saravan state officials a regular salary. [95]

Sasanid society had been divided into four classes: warriors, scribes, priests, and commoners, with the warriors (Arteshtaran) comprising an hereditary elite. Khosrau I (531-579 CE) broke the tradition and admitted a large number of lower nobility called Dehkans into the army. [96]

Seven aristocratic families that had dominated the military and government leadership positions were:

House of Sassan

Aspahbad-Pahlav (Gurgan)

Karin-Pahlav (Shiraz)

Suren-Pahlav (Seistan)

Spandiyadh (Nihavand)

Mihram (Rayy)

Guiw

All except the Sassans were Parthian in origin. [97]

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ absent: 488-531 CE; present: 531-642 CE ♥

Before the reforms of Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) "all nobles, great and small, had been obliged to equip themselves and their followers and serve in the army without pay, but Khusrau issued equipment to the poorer nobles and paid a salary for their services. Consequently, the power of the great nobles - who frequently had their own private armies - was reduced."[98]

"Payment for service might have arisen because of the vast income from silver mines, among other sources." [99]

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ inferred present ♥ Under Shapur I Zoroastrian hierarchy became tied to state. (240-270 CE)[100] code still relevant for this period?

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ {absent; present}: 488-531 CE; {absent; present}: 531-642 CE ♥ Codification of Sasanian law occurred Khusrau I - Khusrau II. [103]

absent

"codified law did not exist in Sasanian Iran" the Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions "cannot be considered a legal code. It is one of the collections that were compiled as manuals for the administration of justice."[104]
Codification of Sasanian law occurred Khusrau I - Khusrau II. [105] i.e. absent before Khurau I.

present

"In the specialist literature, the Madigan has become known as the 'Sasanian Legal Code'. ... It is possible to reconstitute practically the entire system of Iranian law on the basis of the mass of information contained in the Code."[106]
Codification of Sasanian law occurred Khusrau I - Khusrau II. [107]


According to the Dankard, a judge had to consider the Avesta, its Pahlavi translation and commentaries, and "the consesus of the Righteous (ham-dadestanith i wehan)".[108]
The law was based on religion, specifically "the Holy Scripture of the Avesta and its translation and commentaries in Pahlavi".[109]
Court cases judged on Zoroastrian law, unless both parties from another religion. [110] Codification of Sasanian law occurred Khusrau I - Khusrau II c.531 CE. [111]
Link between Iranian law and Zoroastrian religion shown in Madigan-i hazar dadestan [Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions] c620 CE, author "was a contemporary of Khusrau II."[112]

♠ Judges ♣ present ♥

The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[113], whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[114] is unknown. The mowbed were priest judges.[115] Judges known as rads were among other city officials including tax officials who "represented the central government and were responsible to provincial administrators".[116] The king could "pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[117]

♠ Courts ♣ inferred present ♥

"Not obeying an order to appear in court was regarded as an obstruction of justice (azismand)".[118]

The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[119], whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[120] is unknown. The mowbed were priest judges.[121] Judges known as rads were among other city officials including tax officials who "represented the central government and were responsible to provincial administrators".[122] The king could "pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[123]

♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ absent ♥

The law was based on religion, specifically "the Holy Scripture of the Avesta and its translation and commentaries in Pahlavi".[124]

The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[125], whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[126] is unknown. The mowbed were priest judges.[127] Judges known as rads were among other city officials including tax officials who "represented the central government and were responsible to provincial administrators".[128] The king could "pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[129]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥

♠ Special purpose house ♣ present ♥

♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ Dams, irrigation canals. "during the Sassanid era waste water systems were quite common."[130] Emperor Kavad "passed laws to make women common "property." Special shrines and inns were built where women were used for sexual gratification by complete strangers. But it seems that the Iranian nobility responded with hostility."[131]

♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ "From the earliest times [in Babylonia], the flow of water was controlled for agricultural purposes by an elaborate system of canals, sluices, dams, embankments, and dikes."[132] Irrigation canals.[133]

"Dam construction and qanat or tunnel excavation are among the inventions of Iranians. It is written of Shapur I in the necropolis tabloid that Shapur constructed dams over rivers using funds from his treasury to save farmers from drought. Shapur has said, 'In Susa (modern day Khuzestan) I built so many dams to relieve farmers of a need for water."[134]

♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ present ♥ "In the year 326 AD when the city of Susa was destroyed during an earthquake, Shapur ordered it to be rebuilt with all the urban facilities, including water flowing in every house, a sewer system and a laundry in each neighbourhood (Hashami, 2010)."[135]
♠ markets ♣ inferred present ♥ State had market inspectors (wazarbed).[136]
♠ food storage sites ♣ present ♥ Imperial granaries.

♠ Symbolic building ♣ present ♥

Zoroastrian temples. Khosrau II era temple at Kangavar stood on 8m high platform.[137]

At Shapur I's palace at Bishapur ... "mosaics adorning the floor of the Grand Aiwan are in the Syro-Roman style - the dancing girls, musicians and theatrical masks depicted scenes from the Dionysiac cycle."[138]

"The monumental architecture of the rule of Khusrau II (590-628) differs considerably from everything built earlier. The palace of Imarat-i Khusrau near Qasr-i Shirin, although laid out according to the traditional design, was built on an artifical terrace 8m high. The palace in Bisutun was built on a stone platform reminiscent of Persepolis. Colonnades came into use and elements of Achaemenid architectural decoration were copied."[139]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ Have not come across references to theatres or stadiums. However, land owning nobility played polo and tennis, and enjoyed music and singing. This infers specialised architectural structures such as fields, courts and stages and places for viewing. "Their lives were filled with martial training, hunting, feasting, the enjoyment of women, literature, chess, tennis, polo, music, singing and graphic art. The descriptions of early travellers and historians, and the evidence of archaeology, agree in ascribing to the Sassanid monarchy and nobility a dazzling richness of architectural decoration, costume, and jewelery." [140]

"One of the traditional pastimes of the king and nobles was the hunt, for which special preserves or game parks were built."[141] Such scenes are found on Sassanian silverware.[142]

♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ present ♥

"Important events of the reign of each of the Sasanian kings were written down and preserved in the imperial archives, a practice that probably dates from the very beginning of Sasanian rule."[143]

Archives. Scribal schools (dibirstan). [144]

Medical school of Gundi-Shapur founded by Shapur I (241-272 CE) in Susa and remained active at least until c800 CE when they provided the physicians at the court of the Abbasids.[145]

Information[edit]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says: "The principal units of length in the Near Eastern measurement systems were the foot and the cubit, standing in the ratio of 1:1.5. Subdivisions of the foot were its quarter, the palm, and its 16th, the finger. A higher unit, the fathom, consisted of four cubits."[155]
♠ Area ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Volume ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says there was an attempt at harmonization under the Achaemenids. "The dominant units of capacity in the Achaemenid period are defined by a vessel recovered in the excavations at Persepolis. As in the case of weight-units, the reforms of Darius seem to have made an attempt to harmonize the Babylonian and Old Iranian systems."[156] Devices, called water distributors, were used to "measure and calibrate the flow" of water in rivers." [157]
♠ Weight ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says there was a universal standard under the Achaemenids: the standard unit of weight of the shekel "was enforced throughout the Archaemenid Empire by Darius the Great ... around 515 BCE. In this system, the shekel stood at 8.40 grams."[158]
♠ Time ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Geometrical ♣ inferred present ♥

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ Many written documents, such as those listed under Kinds of Written Documents. "The most widespread languages during the Sasanian era were Middle Persian (or Pahlavi), Parthian, Sogdian, Khwarizmian, Khotanese Saka and Bactrian; various texts in these languages are extant."[159]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Pahlavi script, Manichaean script ("variant of the Syriac script"), Parthian script.[160] "The most widespread languages during the Sasanian era were Middle Persian (or Pahlavi), Parthian, Sogdian, Khwarizmian, Khotanese Saka and Bactrian; various texts in these languages are extant."[161]> Six different scripts known.[162] 1. Avestan: invented by Sasanids. Religious use. 2. Comprehensive: possibly for physiognomy and divination (Arab sources). 3. Turned or Cursive: Legal, medicine, philosophy. 4. Correspondence between officials: letters. 5. Common.
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ absent ♥
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ present ♥

Kinds of Written Documents[edit]

♠ Lists, tables, and classifications ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Calendar ♣ inferred present ♥ Zoroastrian calendar.
♠ Sacred Texts ♣ inferred present ♥ Jewish. Christian. Zoroastrian?
♠ Religious literature ♣ present ♥ "Iranians were familiar with Greek philosophy from the Achaemenid period. This acquaintance was deepened in Sasanian times, leading to the influence of Greek philosophy on Zoroastrian religious works."[163] "Translations of, and commentaries upon, the Avesta ... in Middle Persia (also known as Pahlavi), as well as books written on the basis of oral traditions of Avestan material".[164] Zoroastrian priestly writing: "Middle Persian texts." Commentaries on Avesta. Philosophy and debate. Apocalyptic. Didactic. Geographical and epic. Legal. Cultural. Dictionaries. [165] The Original Creation "subject-matter ranges from cosmology, astronomy and eschatology to lists of rivers, mountains and plants."[166] Religious Judgements by Manuchihr. Answered 92 questions on Zoroastrian belief.[167]
♠ Practical literature ♣ present ♥ Manuals for games, like chess (translated from India).[168] "There were several handbooks in Pahlavi dealing with institutions, court manners and ceremonies, the duties of the various social classes, the rules of battle, the arts of warfare (horsemanship and shooting), and games and entertainments (such as polo, chess and backgammon."[169]
♠ History ♣ present ♥ "Important events of the reign of each of the Sasanian kings were written down and preserved in the imperial archives, a practice that probably dates from the very beginning of Sasanian rule."[170] Khwaday-namag, Yazdgird III. History of creation to Khosrau II from a Zoroastrian perspective.[171] "The idea of compiling a written national history for the Iranians appeared toward the end of the Sasanian period, especially at the time of Khusrau I, during whose reign books were either written in Pahlavi or translated from other languages, such as Syriac, the Indian languages and Greek."[172]
♠ Philosophy ♣ present ♥ "Iranians were familiar with Greek philosophy from the Achaemenid period. This acquaintance was deepened in Sasanian times, leading to the influence of Greek philosophy on Zoroastrian religious works."[173] Works translated from Romans.[174] Advice to kings genre: "Several works discussed government policies and ways and means of governing the kingdom. Among them is the Name-i Tansar [Letter of Tansar], written by Tansar (or, in the correct form, Tosar), the Zoroastrian mobad (high priest) at the time of Ardashir I, in response to Gushnasp, king of Tabaristan. ... changes were made to it in later periods, particularly during the reign of Khusrau I.[175]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ present ♥ Astronomy, medicine, mirrors (translated from India).[176] Medical treatises (translated from Romans).[177] "Iranian interest in Indian philosophy and science during the Sasanian period is demonstrated by translations into Middle Persian of Indian works on mathematics, astronomy and medicine, and of belles-lettres and didactic texts".[178] Court doctors, including Indian doctors, suggest Indian medical works likely translated.[179] However, most scientific literature translated was from Syriac and Greek.[180]
♠ Fiction ♣ present ♥ Religious and secular writings but secular writings "written within the framework of Zoroastrian religious beliefs".[181] "Towards the end of the Sasanian period, especially during the reign of Khusrau I (531-579) and later, increasing attention was paid to the task of collecting legends." An Iranian collection of tales "The Thousand Tales" was translated into Arabic and influenced The Thousand and One Nights. Fables and stories translated from India, included Book of Sindbad, Seventy Tales of the Parrot, Kalilag u Dimnag and Bilauhar u Budasaf (which concerned the Buddha). Vis u Ramin a Parthian origin tale and Vamiq u Adhra, a Greek story, were translated into Pahlavi in this period.[182][183] "Epic stories, frequently in verse, remained an oral form until the Sasanian period and some were used in the compilation of the Khwaday-namag [Book of Lords] ... in Pahlavi."[184]


Money[edit]

♠ Articles ♣ present ♥ In local villages.
♠ Tokens ♣ ♥ unknown
♠ Precious metals ♣ ♥ unknown
♠ Foreign coins ♣ inferred present ♥ "Banking was well advanced."[185]
♠ Indigenous coins ♣ present ♥ [186][187] Standardized coins and weights. Gold denar, silver drachm, one sixth silver dang, copper pasiz.[188] "Striking coins was always a royal prerogative, and during the entire Sasanian history the typology employed is the same over the entire empire, proving that the mints always were under control of the royal central authorities."[189] "Sasanan coinage of silver and copper, more rarely of gold, circulated over a wide area".[190] Drachms (fine silver), half-drachms, obols, half-obols, tetradrachms ("poor silver alloy")[191] Khusrau II, later Sassanid period, was the last ruler to issue gold coins.[192]
♠ Paper currency ♣ absent ♥
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ present ♥ "Banking was well advanced."[193] The word "cheque" has a Persian root.[194] Sassanid kings took loans from banks.[195]
♠ Store of wealth ♣ inferred present ♥

Postal System[edit]

♠ Couriers ♣ present ♥ Postal system.[196]
♠ Postal stations ♣ present ♥ "In Persia the postal service appears to have originated in the Achaemenid period. ... There were way stations where the couriers could rest and where fresh horses could be obtained. ... Under the Sasanians a similar postal system appears to have been in operation; in a peace treaty concluded with Byzantium in a.d. 561 one clause stipulated that envoys should be supplied with mounts at the postal stations maintained by both empires."[197] The barid of the Islamic era thought to have been based on earlier system of postal stations.

There were way stations where the couriers could rest and where fresh horses could be obtained. ... Under the Sasanians a similar postal system appears to have been in operation; in a peace treaty concluded with Byzantium in a.d. 561 one clause stipulated that envoys should be supplied with mounts at the postal stations maintained by both empires."[198]

Military Technologies[edit]

Military use of Metals[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ inferred present ♥ bronze is made with copper. Copper weapons present in preceding Parthian polity [199]
♠ Bronze ♣ inferred present ♥ present in preceding Parthian polity [200]
♠ Iron ♣ inferred present ♥ present in preceding Parthian polity [201]
♠ Steel ♣ present ♥ "It is believed that Indian steel was exported in the early centuries A.D. and was known even in the time of Alexander. By the sixth century there is more definite evidence of the manufacture of Damascene swords and the steel used for this purpose came from India."[202] Use of Damascene steel certainly by 540 CE: "This unique type of steel was a major technological innovation and Iran played an important role in its production over the centuries. Circumstantial evidence suggests that a trade in a special steel, conceivably the ingots from which damascene steel was made, was underway in the Parthian and Sasanian period. Sometime after 115 A.D. the Parthians were importing iron (steel) from some point to the east" [203] "High-carbon steel was being produced in the eastern Iranian region from the tenth century CE."[204]

Had a range of 175 meters, accurate within 50-60 meters.[207] "During the reign of the first King Khosrow, or Chosroes (531-79), a cavalryman's equipment consisted of ... a quiver with thirty arrows, two reflex bows, and two replacement strings."[208] at the muster parades of Khusrau I (second Sassanid period) cavalry units required to have "mail, breastplate, helmet, leg guards, arm guards, horse armour, lance, buckler, sword, mace, battle axe, quiver of thirty arrows, bow case with two bows, and two spare bow strings."[209]

In the Islamic world extraordinary distances were shot with this device." [210]

Armor[edit]

♠ Wood, bark, etc ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Leather, cloth ♣ present ♥ "The Persian royal horses are caparisoned, each one bearing rows of badges or symbols. Such trappers could have been of leather or quilted fabric with the devices applied in precious metals."[228]
♠ Shields ♣ present ♥ Mostly used by heavy infantry and foot archers. One-piece leather hide, later wicker-work and rawhide. Paighan siege workers used large shields made of goat wool. Sasanian cavalry did not use large shields. In the later Sasanian Empire a small buckler shield was sometimes worn on the left forearm.[229] "During the reign of the first King Khosrow, or Chosroes (531-79), a cavalryman's equipment consisted of body armor, breastplate, helmet, greaves and arm shields".[230] "The cavalry do not appear to have used shields in the Early Sasanian period."[231]
♠ Helmets ♣ present ♥ Two-piece, ridge helmet and the four-part Spangenhelm. Later designs incorporated mail to protect the face. Other technologies[edit]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present ♥ The Sasanid Spah was a standing army. [256]

♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ inferred present ♥ "Controls were extremely stringent where industry dealt with state organisations such as the army, court and administration."[257]

"From the time of Khosrow's reformation of the army, the king had provided the Sassanid "knights" with horses, equipment, and money, if they possessed no assets themselves. An accurate accounting was kept of weapons, pay, and the conditions of the horses; the "vassals'" armor was stored in the royal armories."[258]

♠ Total army ♣ 183,000 ♥ Khosrau I's army numbered 183,000. 120,000 were Paighen: farmers and labourers used for siege work. 40,000 combat infantry. 23,000 cavalry. Within the cavalry force there was a unit of 10,000 "Immortals." Khosrau I admitted lower nobility into the Saravan ranks increasing its size.

♠ Professional army ♣ 70,000 ♥ 70,000: 531-579 CE military professionals.[259]

Khosrau I's army numbered 183,000. 120,000 were Paighen: farmers and labourers used for siege work. 40,000 combat infantry. 23,000 cavalry. Within the cavalry force there was a unit of 10,000 "Immortals." Khosrau I admitted lower nobility into the Saravan ranks increasing its size.

Under Khosrau II (591-628 CE) there were 50,000 Savaran cavalry in the Sasanid Spah. These were known as the "Golden Spearmen." [260]

♠ Largest fielded army ♣ [12,000-20,000] ♥ Due to the multiple active fronts the Sasanids had to defend they could not bring all their fighting resources (70,000 men) together into one place.[261]

♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥

♠ Sources of recruits ♣ domestic; foreign ♥

Domestic forces made up the core of the Sasanian cavalry. In the earlier Sasanian Empire, Azadan nobility, descendants of ancient Aryan clans, dominated the elite Savaran unit. In the later period, the reforms of Khosrau I (531-579 CE) enabled lower nobility, the Dehkans, into the ranks of the Savaran.[262]

After the reforms of Khosrau I (531-579 CE) foreign contingents were used for horse archers. However, they did not play a significant role in the Sasanid lance fighting system. The Sasanid Spah also contained a force of Armenian elite cavalry. [263]

Nomadic tribes also served in the reformed military, in return for fiefdoms. E.g. Daylamites. They were a separate power-base for the king whose loyalty could be trusted. [264]

Infantry - peasants on military service. Cavalry - nobles, who before Khusrau I were unpaid and often had their own private armies.[265]


♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by soldiers; by state ♥ by soldiers: 488-531 CE; by state: 531-579 CE. Not until Khosrau I (531-579 CE) in the second period of the Sasanid Empire did the army receive horses and equipment from the state.

"The ruler provided armor for his nobles with fiefs in addition to what they themselves supplied."[266]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ Romans, Byzantines, Turks, Hephthalites.

♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥

Intra-elite conflicts. Hamizid IV (579-590 CE), who followed Khosrau I, had many enemies at court, killed many of the nobility and was harsh to the priests.

Hormizd IV deposed 589-590 CE by general and nobility who put on the throne his son, Khusrau II.[267]

Kavad II (628-630 CE) conducted a fratricide, killing all the male heirs in the Sasanid family, and was assassinated.[268]

By 630s CE the empire was in confusion, had disintegrated into regional power-bases and internal conflict when Khuzistan fell to Caliph Umar. Arabs conquered the Sasanid stronghold (Persis) in 650 CE.

♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ In 651 CE Yazdegerd III "murdered at Merv"[269] - possibly on orders of the governor.

The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[270] Hormizd III (457-459 CE) defeated by Peroz (459-484 CE). Jamasp by Kavad I (2nd reign)?Bahram VI (590-591 CE). Vistahm (591-597 CE). Hormizd V (?590 CE). Hormizd VI (631-632 CE) Khusrau IV (632 CE)[271] according to Firdausi's Shah-name, Hormizd IV (579-590 CE) "was imprisoned by his general Bahram Chobin and the nobility in c. 590".[272] Kavad II "committed fratricide, killing every eligible male hier from the Sasanian family."[273] Ardashir III overthrown by non-Sasanian general Shahrbaraz who proclaimed himself King of Kings.[274] Queen Azarmidokht was killed by son of a general killed by the queen.[275]

Archaeological proxies:

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

This suggests skeletons exist but no mention here of violence: "Because of their belief in the sanctity of earth, Zoroastrians did not bury their dead. They believed that death was an act and function of evil; therefore, they left their corpses in an area of high elevation or mountainous region until their flesh had deteriorated and disappeared. This step was tehn followed by the retrieval of the skeletons and burial in a common chamber. Some of these burial chambers can still be found in Iran."[289]

♠ Mass burials ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

Collective burial: "Because of their belief in the sanctity of earth, Zoroastrians did not bury their dead. They believed that death was an act and function of evil; therefore, they left their corpses in an area of high elevation or mountainous region until their flesh had deteriorated and disappeared. This step was tehn followed by the retrieval of the skeletons and burial in a common chamber. Some of these burial chambers can still be found in Iran."[290]

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ "No Roz is the most joyous and beautiful of the Zoroastrian feasts, a spring festival invested with especial religious significance. Religiously it celebrates the creation of fire, which informs all the other six creations, being as it were their spirit or life-force" [292]. It is notoriously difficult to reconstruct the history of Zoroastrian practices [293], but there are records regarding Sasanian kings and their role in Nowruz, as well as their occasional calendrical reforms, which affected the exact time when Nowruz, specifically, was celebrated [294]. It should be noted that there were at least two other very popular Zoroastrian festivals in ancient Iran, Mehragan and Tiragan, but Nowruz was probably somewhat more important, as suggested both by the fact that it was one of the seven annual feasts with mandatory participation [295], and the fact that it was the only major Zoroastrian festival to survive the spread of Islam in the region [296].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [297].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [298].

♠ Duration ♣ 24 ♥ hours on basis ritual is for the whole day one day. [300]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.

♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ No Roz was one of the seven Zoroastrian festivals to demand mandatory participation [302]. The kings also ate on No Roz morning 'white sugar with fresh Indian nuts pared.'" [324].

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Yasna ♥ The Yasna ritual is the central ritual of Zoroastrianism [340]. The fact that the first sacred Zoroastrian texts were compiled during Sasanian times, including the text meant to be recited during the Yasna ritual itself, also known as Yasna, is probably sufficient proof that the ritual was performed in those times. Very briefly, the ritual consisted of the sacrifice of an animal in a specially designated place, as well as the recitation of the sacred text from which the Yasna takes its name.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ [341]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ [342]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ daily ♥ [343]
♠ Duration ♣ [2-3] ♥ hours. two and a half hours The liturgical text from which the Yasna takes its name is recited in full during the ritual, which means that, in modern times, the ritual lasts about two and a half hours [344]. This was probably about the same in ancient times.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 2 ♥ It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [345]. No information is available regarding the number of laypeople present at the ritual. "In the Avesta, several types of priests are listed, but according to the manuscripts, only two were involved in the yasna sacrifice, the principal, or libating, priest (zaotar) and his assistant."[346] "There is no specific term for the assistant (he is called raspi, from unattestd Avestan rathwiya, 'the original one in charge of the ratus'), and he speaks only to answer the libating priest. It is the libating priest who conducted all the formal rituals".[347]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ priests. It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [348]. Not specified by sources--expert input needed."In the Avesta, several types of priests are listed, but according to the manuscripts, only two were involved in the yasna sacrifice, the principal, or libating, priest (zaotar) and his assistant."[349] "There is no specific term for the assistant (he is called raspi, from unattestd Avestan rathwiya, 'the original one in charge of the ratus'), and he speaks only to answer the libating priest. It is the libating priest who conducted all the formal rituals".[350]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ elites ♥ clergy only. Lay people are not allowed to participate in the ritual in modern days [351], which suggests they probably weren't allowed to do so in Sasanian times either, although it seems that, at the very least, they were allowed to spectate, and partake of the sacrificial animal's meat [352].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ Lay people are not allowed to participate in the ritual in modern days [353], which suggests they probably weren't allowed to do so in Sasanian times either, although it seems that, at the very least, they were allowed to spectate, and partake of the sacrificial animal's meat [354]. Not specified by sources--expert input needed.

'Among other things which it was thought propitious to begin the day with, was a mouthful of pure fresh milk and fresh cheese; all the kings of Persia took it as a blessing.' The kings also ate on No Roz morning 'white sugar with fresh Indian nuts pared.'" [518].

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Zoroastrian mortuary ritual ♥ The Zoroastrian ritual of exposing cadavers to the elements, and subsequently to gather the bones and deposit them in ossuaries, is believed to have become "more or less obligatory" under Sasanid rule [534]. Indeed, the most detailed ancient account of this practice dates to Sasanid times: sixth-century CE Byzantine historian Agathias wrote "this is the Persian funeral practice: the flesh is removed in this way and the sposed bones rot, scattered at random all over the plain. They consider it irreligious to place the dead in a tomb or container, or even to bury them in the ground" [535]. Sacred Zoroastrian texts instruct that "[t]he corpse is to be exposed naked, and must be bound to the ground or fastened to it with stones or bricks. The flesh must then be consumed swiftly by birds and dogs" [536]. Indeed, outside observers in ancient times often make specific reference to the dogs and birds to which the Iranian dead were destined [537]. This ritual is classified here as the most dysphoric collective ritual because dead bodies appear to have elicited feelings of fear and disgust in the participants: "Death being regarded as an evil brought about by Aŋra Mainyu (Ahriman), the Destructive Spirit, the corpse of a holy creature, particularly man or dog, is considered to be greatly infested by the druj Nasu, the creature of the Lie called Corpse-matter. Burial of “corpse matter” (nasa-niganih) would defile the earth (in the Sad dar, chap. 33, it is said that Spandārmad shudders when a corpse is buried) and was therefore prohibited" [538].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ in terms of whole polity "daily" in terms of a particular ossuary possibly weekly? frequent Inferred from the fact that this ritual was performed every time somebody died.
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.

Burial of “corpse matter” (nasa-niganih) would defile the earth (in the Sad dar, chap. 33, it is said that Spandārmad shudders when a corpse is buried) and was therefore prohibited" [541].

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[570] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[571] "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials"[572] The Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions contains "passages from official edicts and decrees."[573] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[574] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[575]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ [absent; present] ♥ By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[576] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[577] "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials"[578] The Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions contains "passages from official edicts and decrees."[579] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[580] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[581] The powerful Zoroastrian church and administrative manuals that contain information on the "rights of specific state departments and officials" suggest that the king could not always issue edicts and decrees how he wished and that lawmaking responsibilities were distributed and under the influence most likely of the chief Zoroastrian officials. Whether this was merely the situation de facto or whether it was also considered legal might depend on the personality of the king. The king could "replace" the mowbedan mowbed "and pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[582]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ inferred absent ♥ The centralization reforms of Kavad I and Khusrau I must have taken power away from the priests. However, after Khusrau I the elite relationships appear to be very unstable again.

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sasanid society had four classes: warriors, scribes, priests, and commoners. The warriors (Arteshtaran) were an hereditary elite.[583] Seven aristocratic families dominated the military and government leadership positions. All except the Sassans were Parthian in origin. [584] The warriors (Arteshtaran) were an hereditary elite.[585] Some of the powerful families at the top of the Sasanid state "held hereditary offices, both civil and military".[586] Formal "charters of rank (gah-namag)" showed an individual's precise status in relation to society and the king.[587] Nobles were also graded among themselves.[588] The sahryaran (local dynasties), waspuhragan (princes), wuzurgan (heads of aristocratic families), azadan (other noblement). Membership of one of these noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[589] This was a very rigid caste society with a strong religious hierarchy so it is probably unlikely that a peasant would be able to petition the executive to change the law.
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ present ♥ "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials".[590] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[591] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[592]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ present ♥ Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[593] The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[594] whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[595] is unknown. The king could "replace" the mowbedan mowbed "and pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[596]

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ present ♥ During the infancy of Shapur II (309-379 CE) "the court and the Zoroastrian priests ran an empire that was secure and stable enough structurally and administratively to survive without a strong monarchy. This scenario also signaled to the courtiers and the nobility that the empire could be managed without a powerful king."[597] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[598] "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials"[599] The Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions contains "passages from official edicts and decrees."[600] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[601] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[602] Sasanids agreed to be the guardian of Theodosius II, the son of and successor to the East Roman Emperor Arcadius (383-408 CE). From this time "guardianship of the hiers to the throne of the respective empires" was sometimes requested and granted on both sides. The fact this helped to secure the succession possibly indicates "each were more fearful of internal opposition than of each other."[603] Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[604] An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[605] Fifth century kings were "generally weak, and the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests were able to conduct their activities at the expense of the royal power."[606] The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[607] Head of "seven great families", with lineages from the Achaemenid period, who were feudal rulers. At least before the sixth century fiscal reforms of Khusrau I the peasants paid taxes to them in addition to the Sasanid rulers, and they held both civil and military office.[608]

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ Succession to the throne was determined by the king until nobility and priests became powerful.[621] "The method of election was initially based on the choice of the preceding king, but later the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests took over the decision."[622]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[623] that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[624] The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[625] Fifth century kings "generally weak, and the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests were able to conduct their activities at the expense of the royal power."[626] Kavad I and Khusrau I in this period had long reigns and their centralization reforms must have made the kingship stronger at least for the duration of their reigns.
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ Seven aristocratic families dominated the military and government leadership positions. All except the Sassans were Parthian in origin. Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ While Christians were present (employed?) at the Sasanian court the Zoroastrian clergy "were fiercely intolerant of Christians at all periods, and were restrained only by the political and economic interests of the Sasanian rulers and their circle."[635] "by the end of the Sasanian period there were churches and bishoprics established throughout the empire, and many from the royal family also converted to Christianity."[636]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[637] The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[638] that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[639] The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[640] Fifth century kings "generally weak, and the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests were able to conduct their activities at the expense of the royal power."[641] Kavad I and Khusrau I in this period had long reigns and their centralization reforms must have taken power away from Zoroastrians priests.
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire."[642] The first Sasanian ruler in Istakhr, Pabag, may have succeeded in his revolt having persuaded local warriors "who were devoted to the Zoroastrian goddess. The cult of lady Anathita/Anahid was an old one in Fars, and so religion was used to bring about political control over the Iranian Plateau."[643] Under Shapur II, power of the nobility and priests increased substantially. The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[644] that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). An inscription relates that Ardashar II purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[645] The sophisticated, centralised bureaucracy was now "under the control of the priests" and its chief priest, with Kingship relegated to the status of a secular institution.[646] Ardashir I was the first king. His sons controlled the provinces. Succession to the throne was determined by the king until nobility and priests became powerful. [647] Mazdakite religious movement.[648] 400 CE power of king increased vis-a-vis Zoroastrian priests. Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE) maintained such good relations with the religious minorities that Zoroastrian sources called him a 'sinner' while the Byzantine historian Agathias called him pro-Christian. On his coinage Yazdgerd styled himself as one "who maintains peace in his realm." From his time "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[649] Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[650] The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[651] Fifth century kings "generally weak, and the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests were able to conduct their activities at the expense of the royal power."[652]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ The first Sasanian ruler in Istakhr, Pabag, may have succeeded in his revolt having persuaded local warriors "who were devoted to the Zoroastrian goddess. The cult of lady Anathita/Anahid was an old one in Fars, and so religion was used to bring about political control over the Iranian Plateau."[653] Zoroastrianism was the state religion and its behaviour reflected its "desire to maintain its supremacy. For Iranian Zoroastrians there was an intimate connection between law and religion."[654] Under Shapur I Zoroastrian hierarchy becomes tied to state. (240-270 CE) [655] Under Shapur II, power of the nobility and priests increased substantially. The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[656] that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). An inscription relates that Ardashar II purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[657] The sophisticated, centralised bureaucracy was now "under the control of the priests" and its chief priest, with Kingship relegated to the status of a secular institution.[658] "The Sasanian state used the churches as intermediaries to regulate and tax the population."[659] "Magians had a hierarchy parallel to that of the state, a hierarchical judicial administration specifically for Zoroastrians, a cult, scriptures, religious laws, and distinctive customs. It was the religion of the elite and rulers."[660] 400 CE power of king increased vis-a-vis Zoroastrian priests. Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE) maintained such good relations with the religious minorities that Zoroastrian sources called him a 'sinner' while the Byzantine historian Agathias called him pro-Christian. On his coinage Yazdgerd styled himself as one "who maintains peace in his realm." From his time "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[661]
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ inferred absent ♥ "When Sassanid kings were raised to the throne they received the insignia of royal authority from the chief Mobedh who held the highest religious office."[662] The first Sasanian ruler in Istakhr, Pabag, may have succeeded in his revolt having persuaded local warriors "who were devoted to the Zoroastrian goddess. The cult of lady Anathita/Anahid was an old one in Fars, and so religion was used to bring about political control over the Iranian Plateau."[663] Certainly not in the second period during the reigns of Kavad I and Khusrau I.
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ In Zoroastrian tradition after death bones (asti) and (breath-)soul (urwan) are 'wrenched apart'. The breath-soul is judged at the Ford of the Accountant "where rewards or punishments are meted out according to the total account of the persons good and evil thoughts, words, and deeds." The souls of the rightuous go to a good place - perhaps a House of Songs - while the wicked souls are taken to a bad place e.g. 'Endless Darkness'.[664] The first Sasanian ruler in Istakhr, Pabag, may have succeeded in his revolt having persuaded local warriors "who were devoted to the Zoroastrian goddess. The cult of lady Anathita/Anahid was an old one in Fars, and so religion was used to bring about political control over the Iranian Plateau."[665]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ present ♥ Succession to the throne was determined by the king until nobility and priests became powerful.[666] "The method of election was initially based on the choice of the preceding king, but later the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests took over the decision."[667] Ardashir I was the first king. His sons controlled the provinces. Succession to the throne was determined by the king until nobility and priests became powerful.[668] According to dynastic chart[669], son or brother. Sasanids agreed to be the guardian of Theodosius II, the son of and successor to the East Roman Emperor Arcadius (383-408 CE). From this time "guardianship of the hiers to the throne of the respective empires" was sometimes requested and granted on both sides. This helped to secure the succession.[670] Does not appear to have been one method but there was a general idea that the successor would come from the Sasan family.
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ absent ♥ Succession to the throne was determined by the king until nobility and priests became powerful.[671] "The method of election was initially based on the choice of the preceding king, but later the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests took over the decision."[672] Ardashir I was the first king. His sons controlled the provinces. Succession to the throne was determined by the king until nobility and priests became powerful.[673] According to dynastic chart[674], son or brother. Sasanids agreed to be the guardian of Theodosius II, the son of and successor to the East Roman Emperor Arcadius (383-408 CE). From this time "guardianship of the hiers to the throne of the respective empires" was sometimes requested and granted on both sides. This helped to secure the succession.[675] Does not appear to have been one method but there was a general idea that the successor would come from the Sasan family.

Yazdgerd III (632-651 CE).[676][677] ***Note: have removed Wistahm (maternal uncle of Khosrow II) from the chronology that exists in the 'The Oxford Handbook of Iranian History'[678] because in Daryaee's specialist work there was no overturn, only an on-going transition contest: "His uncle, Wistahm, who had been Khusro's supporter, was targeted by his enemies and so as a result he took himself to Media, minted coins in his own name and probably lived there until 600 CE."[679] +++ In the same work Khosrow II rules briefly before Bahram VI. From the numismatic evidence ..." it seems that the last four in this list "ruled different areas of the empire simultaneously from the end of 631 CE to 637 CE, when Yazdgerd II had already been on the throne for some years."[694] This probably does not count as a successful overturn as other claimants still exist so, effectively, the transition contest is continuing and will continue until there is one left - Yazdgerd III. *632* Khusro III: "We have a list of kings who struck coins and others who are known only from the literary sources, but this era is confusing in terms of succession and only a tentative sequence of rulers can be supplied. Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥

Administrative capital was at Ctesiphon.[718] Increase in bureaucracy under Shapur I.[719] By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[720] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[721] Secretaries of a divan (ministry)[722] Scribes were called dirbirs.[723] Treasurers called ganzwars.[724] There was a central archives: "Important events of the reign of each of the Sasanian kings were written down and preserved in the imperial archives, a practice that probably dates from the very beginning of Sasanian rule."[725] The city of Bishapur, founded by Shapur I c262 CE, had a Greek plan,[726] which perhaps shows skills and resources inherited from Greek administrators who were common under Parthian rule. The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[727] (That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[728]).


♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower: 488-530 CE; equal: 531-646 CE ♥ The reforms of Khusrau I increased centralization and the status of state officials so that, for example, for the first time "all the taxes were in the hands of the king."[729] .

Administrative capital was at Ctesiphon.[730] Increase in bureaucracy under Shapur I.[731] Under Shapur II, power of the nobility and priests increased substantially.[732] By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[733] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ widespread: 488-530 CE; significant: 531-579 CE; widespread: 580-646 CE ♥ according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) the Sassanid elite claimed "the power of life and death over slaves and commons."[754] An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[755] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[756] We might also conclude from this behaviour that the bureaucrats within the central administration, coming from the outside and dependent on the king, would have been more loyal to the king. On the other hand, the fact the king tried hard to do this suggests there may have been problems with officials recruited from the dominant families.

♠ Abuse of power ♣ widespread: 488-530 CE; significant: 531-579 CE; widespread: 580-646 CE ♥ according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) the Sassanid elite claimed "the power of life and death over slaves and commons."[757] An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[758] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[759] We might also conclude from this behaviour that the bureaucrats within the central administration, coming from the outside and dependent on the king, would have been more loyal to the king. On the other hand, the fact the king tried hard to do this suggests there may have been problems with officials recruited from the dominant families.
