Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ suspected unknown ♥

The system of regional government was mixed. Ardashir I was the first King of Kings and his sons controlled the provinces. There were vassal kingdoms of "the great and petty princes who, for recognizing and being loyal to Persian overlordship, were allowed to retain possession of their thrones."[764] There were annexed territories governed by "princes of the royal blood" who also could run provinces.[765] Vassal kingdoms (shahrs) often ruled by the son of the monarch. Vassals included Merv, Kerman, Sakastan, Adiabene, Iberia, Makran, Mesene, Kushanshahr and Armenia.[766] (Are these the viceroys called bidakhsh[767]?) Provinces were run by the heads of "seven great families", whose lineages dated back to the Achaemenid period. Peasants paid taxes to these fuedal rulers in addition to the Sasanid rulers, and they held both civil and military office.[768] The seven families "provided the Crown with military support and were obligated to levy troops, if necessity arose."[769] The administration in provinces, and districts below them, "did not differ greatly from that under the Parthians."[770] Within provinces were districts.[771] Districts had divisions called 'rustag' (which covered a number of villages). These divisions were divided into 'deh' (village) which were headed by Deghan.[772] Governors called shahrabs[773] headed the provinces.[774] (In the early period "royal cities, almost equivalent to semi-independent kingdoms, were built" administered by a shahrab.[775]). "The cities of Sasanian Iran ... had district inspectors and there is some evidence that districts were enclosed by walls."[776] "At the bottom of the hierarchy were the freemen, the small landed nobility or headmen of villages (Dekhans), the link between the peasant and the official representative of the central government. They were responsible to the administration for the collection of taxes levied on the peasants, who formed the great mass of the population."[777]


♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥

The system of regional government was mixed. There were vassal kingdoms of "the great and petty princes who, for recognizing and being loyal to Persian overlordship, were allowed to retain possession of their thrones."[778] There were annexed territories governed by "princes of the royal blood" who also could run provinces.[779] Vassal kingdoms (shahrs) often ruled by the son of the monarch. Vassals included Merv, Kerman, Sakastan, Adiabene, Iberia, Makran, Mesene, Kushanshahr and Armenia.[780] (Are these the viceroys called bidakhsh[781]?) Provinces were run by the heads of "seven great families", whose lineages dated back to the Achaemenid period. Peasants paid taxes to these fuedal rulers in addition to the Sasanid rulers, and they held both civil and military office.[782] The seven families "provided the Crown with military support and were obligated to levy troops, if necessity arose."[783] The administration in provinces, and districts below them, "did not differ greatly from that under the Parthians."[784] Within provinces were districts.[785] Districts had divisions called 'rustag' (which covered a number of villages). These divisions were divided into 'deh' (village) which were headed by Deghan.[786] Governors called shahrabs[787] headed the provinces.[788] (In the early period "royal cities, almost equivalent to semi-independent kingdoms, were built" administered by a shahrab.[789]). "The cities of Sasanian Iran ... had district inspectors and there is some evidence that districts were enclosed by walls."[790] "At the bottom of the hierarchy were the freemen, the small landed nobility or headmen of villages (Dekhans), the link between the peasant and the official representative of the central government. They were responsible to the administration for the collection of taxes levied on the peasants, who formed the great mass of the population."[791]

Centralization occurred in the later Sassanid period when the empire was split into four parts each ruled by a spahbad who had civil and military powers.[792] The late Sasanid Empire was "beginning to resemble the Parthian feudal system before the fall of the Parthians."[793]

Non-Persian communities

An exilarch was the civic cheiftain officer for the Jewish community. He collected taxes and represented the Jews at the imperial court.[794] From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline." [795]


Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ inferred absent ♥ "At the bottom of the hierarchy were the freemen, the small landed nobility or headmen of villages (Dekhans), the link between the peasant and the official representative of the central government. They were responsible to the administration for the collection of taxes levied on the peasants, who formed the great mass of the population."[796] After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "The taxes were collected according to the administrative sub-divisions of the country from village up to province, with the officer in charge of a province being responsible for overall supervision and the tax in each city being paid to the judge of that city."[797] Khusrau I's reforms in the later Sasanid period centralized the tax system and put the collection of taxes in the hands of officials. Before his time tax collection was therefore less centralized; apparently under the control of the landed nobility.
♠ Indirect ♣ present ♥ Provinces were run by the heads of "seven great families". Peasants paid taxes to these fuedal rulers in addition to the Sasanid rulers, and they held both civil and military office.[798] The families who ran the provinces collected tax for themselves as well as the central government so presumably their administrators were responsible to them. After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "The taxes were collected according to the administrative sub-divisions of the country from village up to province, with the officer in charge of a province being responsible for overall supervision and the tax in each city being paid to the judge of that city."[799] Khusrau I's reforms in the later Sasanid period centralized the tax system and put the collection of taxes in the hands of officials.
♠ Diffused ♣ present ♥ Harmatta (1969) suggests that "the western part of the former Kushan Empire became a vassal kingdom under the Sasanians, and subsequently a province governed by the Sasanian price governors. These prince-governors issued coins as Kushanshahs (kings of the Kushans)."[800] "There were sizeable Jewish communities in the towns of Mesopotamia and Iran: that in Iran was self-governing and was generally not persecuted by the Sasanians."[801]
♠ Mixed ♣ present ♥ Harmatta (1969) suggests that "the western part of the former Kushan Empire became a vassal kingdom under the Sasanians, and subsequently a province governed by the Sasanian price governors. These prince-governors issued coins as Kushanshahs (kings of the Kushans)."[802] "There were sizeable Jewish communities in the towns of Mesopotamia and Iran: that in Iran was self-governing and was generally not persecuted by the Sasanians."[803] at local level dehqans "became the important officials and local matters were more important for the local population than the political affairs of the empire."[804]

♠ State salary ♣ inferred present ♥ Before the reforms of Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) "all nobles, great and small, had been obliged to equip themselves and their followers and serve in the army without pay, but Khusrau issued equipment to the poorer nobles and paid a salary for their services. Consequently, the power of the great nobles - who frequently had their own private armies - was reduced."[805]

Pathologies

♠ Abuse of power ♣ pervasive: 488-530 CE; widespread: 531-579 CE; pervasive: 580-646 CE ♥ Khusrau I's reforms in the later Sasanid period centralized the tax system and put the collection of taxes in the hands of officials. Before this time the nobility presumably had a great influence over the amount of funds that would reach the central authorities.

♠ Professionalization of local bureaucracy ♣ inferred present ♥ Judges known as rads were among other city officials including tax officials who "represented the central government and were responsible to provincial administrators".[807] Provinces were run by the heads of "seven great families". Peasants paid taxes to these fuedal rulers in addition to the Sasanid rulers, and they held both civil and military office.[808] There would have been a large administrative infrastructure at the provincial level as was the case during the Parthian period.

♠ modal number of years local bureaucrats spend in service ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ present ♥

Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[809]

"Parsi-speaking Zoroastrians ruled Iraq, but the local populations were Aramaic-speaking Nestorians and Jews" [810] "There were sizeable Jewish communities in the towns of Mesopotamia and Iran: that in Iran was self-governing and was generally not persecuted by the Sasanians."[811] Jews had their own law courts, schools and synagogues.[812]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ inferred present ♥

The law was "Zoroastrianized"[813] based on "the Holy Scripture of the Avesta and its translation and commentaries in Pahlavi".[814] The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[815], whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[816] is unknown. The mowbed were priest judges.[817] Judges known as rads were among other city officials including tax officials who "represented the central government and were responsible to provincial administrators".[818] "Not obeying an order to appear in court was regarded as an obstruction of justice (azismand)".[819]

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ suspected unknown ♥

"Sasanian law does not distinguish theoretically between civil and criminal law."[820]

Accessibility of the Legal System

♠ ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ From the 4th century: "religious communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances".[821] The courts were likely present whereever there was a Zoroastrian, Nestorian Christian or Jewish religious community, i.e. in the towns and cities.
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ inferred present ♥ From the 4th century: "religious communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances".[822] The courts were likely present whereever there was a Zoroastrian, Nestorian Christian or Jewish religious community, i.e. in the towns and cities.
♠ general population ♣ inferred present ♥ From the 4th century: "religious communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances".[823] The courts were likely present whereever there was a Zoroastrian, Nestorian Christian or Jewish religious community, i.e. in the towns and cities.
♠ foreign groups ♣ inferred present ♥ From the 4th century: "religious communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances".[824] Slaves had "certain civil rights; the law not only protected their lives (Pahlavi Rivayat, ed. Dhabhar, p. 108) but also provided them with recourse in defense of their rights".[825] The courts were likely present whereever there was a Zoroastrian, Nestorian Christian or Jewish religious community, i.e. in the towns and cities.
♠ ethnic groups ♣ inferred present ♥ From the 4th century: "religious communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances".[826] Slaves had "certain civil rights; the law not only protected their lives (Pahlavi Rivayat, ed. Dhabhar, p. 108) but also provided them with recourse in defense of their rights".[827]The courts were likely present whereever there was a Zoroastrian, Nestorian Christian or Jewish religious community, i.e. in the towns and cities.

Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ ♥

"Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[838]

Jews had their own law courts, schools and synagogues.[839]

♠ Religious judiciary ♣ present ♥

Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[840] "For Iranian Zoroastrians there was an intimate connection between law and religion."[841] The law was "Zoroastrianized"[842] based on "the Holy Scripture of the Avesta and its translation and commentaries in Pahlavi".[843] The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[844], whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[845] is unknown. The mowbed were priest judges.[846] Judges known as rads were among other city officials including tax officials who "represented the central government and were responsible to provincial administrators".[847] The king could "pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[848] The link between Iranian law and Zoroastrian religion is shown in the Madigan-i hazar dadestan [Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions] c620 CE. Its author "was a contemporary of Khusrau II."[849]

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ present ♥ From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline." [850] Sasanid jurists distinguished between ownership and possession (which might not include ownership i.e. leasehold, tenancy etc. or stolen).[851]

♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[852] Usufruct existed.[853]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ inferred present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[854]
♠ Partibility ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[855] Joint-property existed.[856]
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[857] Sasanid jurists distinguished between ownership and possession (which might not include ownership i.e. leasehold, tenancy etc. or stolen).[858]
♠ Lease Rights ♣ inferred present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[859] Sasanid jurists distinguished between ownership and possession (which might not include ownership i.e. leasehold, tenancy etc. or stolen).[860] A lease could be worded in a contract[861] which suggests that the contract had legal standing.
♠ Transfer of property ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[862] "A land certificate document belonging to the estate, in which all the assets of the premises were set down exactly, including slaves and animals who worked on the land and belonged to the estate, was also transmitted to the new holder or owner (Macuch, 1981, p. 11, and 1999)."[863]
♠ Use of property ♣ ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[864] Sasanid jurists distinguished between ownership and possession (which might not include ownership i.e. leasehold, tenancy etc. or stolen).[865]
♠ Property market ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[866] Sasanid jurists distinguished between ownership and possession (which might not include ownership i.e. leasehold, tenancy etc. or stolen).[867] "A land certificate document belonging to the estate, in which all the assets of the premises were set down exactly, including slaves and animals who worked on the land and belonged to the estate, was also transmitted to the new holder or owner (Macuch, 1981, p. 11, and 1999)."[868]

♠ Other property ownership ♣ ♥ After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "Supervision of the payment of taxes into the royal treasury was undertaken by the hamarkar (accountant), who was also responsible for issuing documents on the right of ownership and possession."[869]

Inheritance System

♠ partible inheritance ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[870] Legal texts (such as the Madigan-i hazar dadestan - Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions) contain "legal cases concerning marriage, inheritance, ownership, endowments ... probably compiled at the time of Khusrau II, but its final redaction belongs to the ninth century."[871] Direct successorship went from fathers to legitimate sons: inherited his property, "his name and place in the descent group, his standing in the community, and his rank in the social estate."[872] Intermediary successorship went from fathers to legitimate daughters who were entitled to "one-half of a son's share" which could be awarded as dowry if she married outside of the descent group.[873]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[874] Direct successorship went from fathers to legitimate sons: inherited his property, "his name and place in the descent group, his standing in the community, and his rank in the social estate."[875] Intermediary successorship went from fathers to legitimate daughters who were entitled to "one-half of a son's share" which could be awarded as dowry if she married outside of the descent group.[876]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ absent ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[877] "A woman was entitled to a share of her father's estate when she married but not to any additional inheritance when he died."[878] Direct successorship went from fathers to legitimate sons: inherited his property, "his name and place in the descent group, his standing in the community, and his rank in the social estate."[879] Intermediary successorship went from fathers to legitimate daughters who were entitled to "one-half of a son's share" which could be awarded as dowry if she married outside of the descent group.[880]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ inferred absent ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[881] "Sasanian law did not know the legal concept of primogeniture (there was no primus agnatus as in Roman law)"; all legitimate children could inherit.[882]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ inferred absent ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[883] "Sasanian law did not know the legal concept of primogeniture (there was no primus agnatus as in Roman law)"; all legitimate children could inherit.[884]
♠ other inheritance practices ♣ inferred present ♥ Sasanid inheritance practices could get very complex. There was a "rule that no man should die without leaving at least one male legal successor" and when that happened there were various solutions to "securing a man’s male offspring."[885] Intermediary successorship went from fathers to legitimate daughters who were entitled to "one-half of a son's share" which could be awarded as dowry if she married outside of the descent group.[886]
♠ Trusts ♣ inferred present ♥ "In the Sassanian endowment, the trustee-heirs share the endowment income only to the extent they are surplus to the need for funding the religious ceremonies."[887] "In a study of Sassanian endowments and the Islamic waqf, Dr. A. Perikhanian ... noted that 'there can now be no doubt of the Iranian origin of the Muslim waqf.'"[888] I don't know whether this assertion is challenged because on Google Books the next page is not available.

♠ Non-state expropriation ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage

♠ Monogamy ♣ inferred present ♥ Especially among Christians Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[889] "Next-of-kin marriage assumed increasing importance in Zoroastrian teachings during later Sasanian and early Islamic periods. Religious leaders encouraged this form of marriage on the grounds that it emulated the act of creation ... Priests argued that marriage within the family produced stronger males, more virtuous females, and higher quality and quantity of children, and it protected the purity of the race and propagated it. ... The defense of khevtuk-das by religious leaders, the increase in rewards promised to anyone who practiced it, and the rise in conversion of Zoroastrians to Christianity may indicate the unpopularity of this marriage practice".[890] However, Zoroastrian emphasis on incentuous marriage mainly later text. Earlier whilst marriage "with non-Iranians was discouraged" the "absence of mention of next-of-kin marriage is striking." this reference refers to an earlier period but may still be valid for this period.[891]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ Note that we cannot infer backwards without difficulty from Umayyad codes: there were "age-old Zoroastrian customs, including especially those laws on marriage, family, and inheritance which differed basically from the new regulations of Islam."[892] according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) "Each man according to his means contracts many or few marriages, whence their affection, divided as it is among various objects, grows cold."[893] "Next-of-kin marriage assumed increasing importance in Zoroastrian teachings during later Sasanian and early Islamic periods. Religious leaders encouraged this form of marriage on the grounds that it emulated the act of creation ... Priests argued that marriage within the family produced stronger males, more virtuous females, and higher quality and quantity of children, and it protected the purity of the race and propagated it. ... The defense of khevtuk-das by religious leaders, the increase in rewards promised to anyone who practiced it, and the rise in conversion of Zoroastrians to Christianity may indicate the unpopularity of this marriage practice".[894] However, Zoroastrian emphasis on incentuous marriage mainly later text. Earlier whilst marriage "with non-Iranians was discouraged" the "absence of mention of next-of-kin marriage is striking." this reference refers to an earlier period but may still be valid for this period.[895]
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ present ♥ according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) "most of them are extravagantly given to venery, and are hardly contented with a multitude of concubines".[896]
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ present ♥ according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) "most of them are extravagantly given to venery, and are hardly contented with a multitude of concubines".[897]

Legal rights are different on the basis of:

♠ Gender ♣ present ♥ By the late Sasanid period "many sources attest the division of society into four estates (pesag), by which not only a person's rank, grade, and position in the State, but also his legal status, were defined."[898] These were the priests (asronan, inc. judges), the warriors (artestaran, i.e. nobility), scribes (dibiran i.e. bureaucracy) and the cultivators (wastaryosan and hutuxsan)."[899] The Zoroastrian law in theory applied to all Zoroastrians equally but Sasanian jurists idea of a 'legal personality' unlikely enforced "equal rights regardless of race, gender, religious beliefs, and status in the community.On the contrary, the legal rights and duties of individuals were defined by their position in society".[900] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[901] "Women were told that their salvation depended no on good works but on total obedience to their husbands. ... Women were told that they lacked wisdom and that their judgment was weaker than men's. For this reason, women's testimony in a court of law was not acceptable."[902]
♠ Social class ♣ present ♥ By the late Sasanid period "many sources attest the division of society into four estates (pesag), by which not only a person's rank, grade, and position in the State, but also his legal status, were defined."[903] These were the priests (asronan, inc. judges), the warriors (artestaran, i.e. nobility), scribes (dibiran i.e. bureaucracy) and the cultivators (wastaryosan and hutuxsan)."[904] The Zoroastrian law in theory applied to all Zoroastrians equally but Sasanian jurists idea of a 'legal personality' unlikely enforced "equal rights regardless of race, gender, religious beliefs, and status in the community.On the contrary, the legal rights and duties of individuals were defined by their position in society".[905] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[906]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ present ♥ By the late Sasanid period "many sources attest the division of society into four estates (pesag), by which not only a person's rank, grade, and position in the State, but also his legal status, were defined."[907] These were the priests (asronan, inc. judges), the warriors (artestaran, i.e. nobility), scribes (dibiran i.e. bureaucracy) and the cultivators (wastaryosan and hutuxsan)."[908] The Zoroastrian law in theory applied to all Zoroastrians equally but Sasanian jurists idea of a 'legal personality' unlikely enforced "equal rights regardless of race, gender, religious beliefs, and status in the community.On the contrary, the legal rights and duties of individuals were defined by their position in society".[909] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[910]

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ [absent; present] ♥ By the late Sasanid period "many sources attest the division of society into four estates (pesag), by which not only a person's rank, grade, and position in the State, but also his legal status, were defined."[911] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[912] Probably due to periodic menstrual bleeding, which was considered polluting and possessed by demons, women were not permitted to perform certain ritual duties, such as tending to the fire. However, she could proselytize the faith. this reference refers to an earlier period but may still be valid for this period.[913] In the towns it was unlikely that women worked due to husbands not wanting them to meet male strangers. Household that could afford it employed slaves and servants for domestic work.[914] Peasant women were probably engaged in agriculture, spun wool and were involved in handicraft production.[915]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to both males and females ♣ [absent; present] ♥ By the late Sasanid period "many sources attest the division of society into four estates (pesag), by which not only a person's rank, grade, and position in the State, but also his legal status, were defined."[916] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[917] Probably due to periodic menstrual bleeding, which was considered polluting and possessed by demons, women were not permitted to perform ritual duties, such as tending to the fire. However, she could proselytize the faith. this reference refers to an earlier period but may still be valid for this period.[918] In the towns it was unlikely that women worked due to husbands not wanting them to meet male strangers. Household that could afford it employed slaves and servants for domestic work.[919]
♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ inferred present ♥ "the Ain-namag [Book of Rules], describes the customs, morals and behaviour prescribed for kings, aristocrats and other high echelons of society."[920] Women could proselytize the faith. this reference refers to an earlier period but may still be valid for this period.[921] Bartholomae tells a story of five Sasanid women who met a judge on a country road. In a discussion on law he was able to answer all their questions except one, prompting the response: "'Master, do not trouble your brain, and say, 'I do not know.' You can find the answer in such and such a work.' The incident reveals that at least some women received an education."[922]
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ [absent; present] ♥ The Zoroastrian law in theory applied to all Zoroastrians equally but Sasanian jurists idea of a 'legal personality' unlikely enforced "equal rights regardless of race, gender, religious beliefs, and status in the community.On the contrary, the legal rights and duties of individuals were defined by their position in society".[923] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[924] Females could probably own property but no reference exactly stating this yet. In the Later Sasanid period there were two female Sasanid monarchs. Check Nashat (2003)[925] However, men had guardianship over women.[926]


Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent ♥ By the late Sasanid period "many sources attest the division of society into four estates (pesag), by which not only a person's rank, grade, and position in the State, but also his legal status, were defined."[927] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[928] Membership of one of the noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[929]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent ♥ By the late Sasanid period "many sources attest the division of society into four estates (pesag), by which not only a person's rank, grade, and position in the State, but also his legal status, were defined."[930] To have full rights one had to be a free born adult male, subject of the king of kings, a citizen of Eransahr, hold to the Zorastrian religion and belong to a noble family."[931] Membership of one of the noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[932]
♠ Education is available to people from all social classes ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Children of the nobility started school at the age of five to seven years and lasted until age fifteen. General education was composed of reading, writing, religious instruction, physical education, and courtly arts. Education included not only hunting and the arts of war but social manners as well."[933] "In the field of education, a short extant treatise entitled the Khweskarih-i Redagan [The Duties of Children] contains instructions concerning children's obligations towards their parents and teachers."[934] Jews had their own law courts, schools and synagogues.[935] Scribal schools (dibirstan).[936] no mention of schools for peasants. unlikely since most peasants were iliterate and "de facto reduced to the condition of serfs attached to the soil, and were sold along with the land and villages."[937]
♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ inferred present ♥ In theory, but in practice the peasants were serfs "de facto reduced to the condition of serfs attached to the soil, and were sold along with the land and villages."[938]

Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race

♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ present ♥ Membership of one of the noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[939] However:"The Sasanian secular administration showed an extremely pragmatic attitude toward the Christians, harsh repression coexisting with the presence of Christians at court and even their acceptance as marriage partners."[940] "When an Iranian doctor was available, it was considered wrong to consult one of foreign origin; despite this, Sasanian kings often preferred to use Greek doctors or Syrian Christians."[941] "all the way to the Abbasid period ... Persian Buddhist families such as the Barmakids functioned as ministers and administrators."[942]

♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ inferred present ♥ However, slaves were considered property and they could not be sold to non-Zoroastrians[950] so this may indicate only the Zoroastrian population possessed them.

Social Mobility[edit]

Status[edit]

Elite status

♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ Sasanid society had four classes: warriors, scribes, priests, and commoners. The warriors (Arteshtaran) were an hereditary elite.[951] Seven aristocratic families dominated the military and government leadership positions. All except the Sassans were Parthian in origin. [952] The warriors (Arteshtaran) were an hereditary elite.[953] Some of the powerful families at the top of the Sasanid state "held hereditary offices, both civil and military".[954] Formal "charters of rank (gah-namag)" showed an individual's precise status in relation to society and the king.[955] Nobles were also graded among themselves.[956] The sahryaran (local dynasties), waspuhragan (princes), wuzurgan (heads of aristocratic families), azadan (other noblement). Membership of one of these noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[957] "Next-of-kin marriage assumed increasing importance in Zoroastrian teachings during later Sasanian and early Islamic periods. Religious leaders encouraged this form of marriage on the grounds that it emulated the act of creation ... Priests argued that marriage within the family produced stronger males, more virtuous females, and higher quality and quantity of children, and it protected the purity of the race and propagated it".[958]
♠ elite status is selected ♣ {absent; present} ♥ The Sasanid "social structure was relatively rigid and formalized. Officially, at least, clear distinctions were made between different social classes ... and there was seemingly little social mobility between them. It is, however, possible that the structure was not so rigid, in practice. Many cases of an individual rising from one class to another (especially in the higher and middle groups) are known; and the kings themselves seem to have encouraged these individuals, through the system of honors they distributed."[959] "When an Iranian doctor was available, it was considered wrong to consult one of foreign origin; despite this, Sasanian kings often preferred to use Greek doctors or Syrian Christians."[960] Membership of a noble grade of rank was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[961]
♠ formal system of hereditary social status ♣ present ♥ Formal "charters of rank (gah-namag)" showed an individual's precise status in relation to society and the king.[962] Nobles were also graded by rank.[963] These were the sahryaran (local dynasties), waspuhragan (princes), wuzurgan (heads of aristocratic families) and azadan (other noblement). Membership of one of these noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[964] However, there were many cases of upward mobility in the higher and middle groups "and the kings themselves seem to have encouraged these individuals, through the system of honors they distributed."[965] "Next-of-kin marriage assumed increasing importance in Zoroastrian teachings during later Sasanian and early Islamic periods. Religious leaders encouraged this form of marriage on the grounds that it emulated the act of creation ... Priests argued that marriage within the family produced stronger males, more virtuous females, and higher quality and quantity of children, and it protected the purity of the race and propagated it".[966]

♠ social mobility ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Sasanid "social structure was relatively rigid and formalized. Officially, at least, clear distinctions were made between different social classes ... and there was seemingly little social mobility between them. It is, however, possible that the structure was not so rigid, in practice. Many cases of an individuals rising from one class to another (especially in the higher and middle groups) are known; and the kings themselves seem to have encouraged these individuals, through the system of honors they distributed."[967] The class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[968] Formal "charters of rank (gah-namag)" showed an individual's precise status in relation to society and the king.[969] It was "almost impossible to change from one estate to another".[970] Nobles were also graded among themselves.[971] The sahryaran (local dynasties), waspuhragan (princes), wuzurgan (heads of aristocratic families), azadan (other noblement). Membership of one of these noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[972] Although from the mid-fifth century the position of the middle and lower classes were improved by state reforms "the estate barriers were made impregnable and the channels of vertical mobility, which were already very narrow, were frozen."[973] inferred absent in this period

♠ recruitment of chief executive ♣ higher ♥
♠ extreme status elevation ♣ absent ♥
♠ extreme status diminution ♣ absent ♥

♠ occupational mobility ♣ inferred present ♥ Mostly within classes/estates rather than between, but there was some movement between middle and higher classes often encouraged by the king.

"Doctors possessed a type of licence authorizing them to engage in medical practice."[974] There was "a relatively strong and wealthy bourgeois class" which included merchants and "heads of industrial workshops".[975]

The Sasanid "social structure was relatively rigid and formalized. Officially, at least, clear distinctions were made between different social classes ... and there was seemingly little social mobility between them. It is, however, possible that the structure was not so rigid, in practice. Many cases of an individuals rising from one class to another (especially in the higher and middle groups) are known; and the kings themselves seem to have encouraged these individuals, through the system of honors they distributed."[976]

The class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[977]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ [5-10] ♥ The majority of the population were de facto enserfed peasants[978] so this is not going to be a high number. Sasanid society also included small landed nobility, merchants, professionals (scribes, doctors, astrologers), priests, warriors and the greater nobility. Slaves could not be sold to non-Zoroastrians[979] so presumably this indicates only the Zoroastrian population possessed them.

Slavery was more widespread in the Sasanid period. There were different categories of slave: bandag (servant); ansahrig (outlander); wardag (captive). The servant was usually in the household or a fire temple. The ansahrig usually worked on a farm and was non-Zoroastrian. A wardag worked on the land, in construction or other manual labor.[980]

"Slavery was hereditary (Madayan, pt. 1, pp. 1, 96), and, according to the jurist Syawaxs, against Rad-Hormizd, no Persian subject (bandag i sahansah) could be enslaved (Madayan, pt. 1, p. 20)."[981]

In Sasanid times "slaves had apparently become the chief producers in society. They were bought or captured by the hundreds (Ammianus Marcellinus, 23.6.76) and were classed as property".[982] Did slavery decrease across the period? Individuals could be bought out of slavery.

A slave could be partially freed in which state "he was paid wages proportional to his degree of freedom."[983]

"If a Christian slave embraced Zoroastrianism and took to the people of the good religion (hu-denan) it was incumbent upon them to buy him out of slaver; but if he did not take to the Zoroastrians, he could procure his own manumission (Madayan, pt. 1, p. 1)." [984]


♠ Proportion of population enserfed ♣ [60-70] ♥ This number refers to peasants who were de facto enserfed. Presumably (?) the cultivators known as wastaryosan and hutuxsan.[985] Sasanid society also included slaves, small landed nobility, merchants, professionals (scribes, doctors, astrologers), priests, warriors and the greater nobility.

"At the bottom of the hierarchy were the freemen, the small landed nobility or headmen of villages (Dekhans), the link between the peasant and the official representative of the central government. They were responsible to the administration for the collection of taxes levied on the peasants, who formed the great mass of the population. Though free de jure, the peasants were de facto reduced to the condition of serfs attached to the soil, and were sold along with the land and villages."[986] There was "a relatively strong and wealthy bourgeois class" which included merchants and "heads of industrial workshops".[987] Could a peasant become bourgeois? ... The Sasanid "social structure was relatively rigid and formalized. Officially, at least, clear distinctions were made between different social classes ... and there was seemingly little social mobility between them. It is, however, possible that the structure was not so rigid, in practice. Many cases of an individual rising from one class to another (especially in the higher and middle groups) are known; and the kings themselves seem to have encouraged these individuals, through the system of honors they distributed."[988] ... the social mobility Eisenstadt speaks of is between the higher and middle groups, not the peasantry.

♠ Corvée labor to a landowner ♣ inferred absent ♥ "At the bottom of the hierarchy were the freemen, the small landed nobility or headmen of villages (Dekhans), the link between the peasant and the official representative of the central government. They were responsible to the administration for the collection of taxes levied on the peasants, who formed the great mass of the population. Since the peasants were de jure free one might conclude that the landowners could not draft them as corvee labor. Slaves, not peasant-serfs, appear to have been the work horses. Slave masters were permitted to treat slaves like draft animals, and slaves had become "the chief producers in society".[1,000]
♠ Corvée labor to the state ♣ inferred absent ♥ "According to tradition, Shapur I used Roman prisoners of war to build dams and bridges in Mesopotamia and Khuzistan."[1,001] no mention of corvee labor in the literature and if the King is using prisoners of war for large public works that indicates he either does not need or may not have the ability to recruit free labor from other sources.


Restraints

♠ Owners can execute slaves ♣ {absent; present} ♥ according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) the Sassanid elite claimed "the power of life and death over slaves and commons. They flay men alive, either bit by bit or all at once, and no servant who waits upon them, or stands at table, is allowed to open his mouth, either to speak or to spit; to such a degree, after the skins are spread, are the mouths of all fettered."[1,002] Note that Ammianus Marcellinus is only one source and a Roman one at that so perhaps there could be political bias here - reporting one case as if it was a general feature of the Sasanid elites. however, we might ask whether the early Sasanid kings claimed "the power of life and death over slaves and commons" because they considered themselves "from the lineage of the gods", "of the race of the gods", the "earthly incarnation of the supreme deity"? The idea among the Sasanid elite must have been powerful because it was around even in the early seventh century when Queen Boran attempted, against the ideology of the Zoroastrian authorities, to be the "restorer of her lineage, the race of gods". in coding this variable, therefore, we need to know whether Ammianus Marcellinus refers to a secret ritual or a punishment. This is a question for an expert. Slave masters were permitted to treat slaves like draft animals.[1,003] However, "they did have certain civil rights; the law not only protected their lives (Pahlavi Rivayat, ed. Dhabhar, p. 108) but also provided them with recourse in defense of their rights".[1,004]
♠ Owners can impose corporal punishment on slaves ♣ inferred present ♥ Slave masters were permitted to treat slaves like draft animals.[1,005] However, "they did have certain civil rights; the law not only protected their lives (Pahlavi Rivayat, ed. Dhabhar, p. 108) but also provided them with recourse in defense of their rights".[1,006]
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ present ♥ "there is plenty of evidence that, besides fines, in criminal cases also corporal punishment was executed" with the punishment intended to "[stigmatize] the delinquent visibly" e.g. mutilation, maiming, branding.[1,007] Slave masters were permitted to treat slaves like draft animals.[1,008] However, "they did have certain civil rights; the law not only protected their lives (Pahlavi Rivayat, ed. Dhabhar, p. 108) but also provided them with recourse in defense of their rights".[1,009] Ammianus Marcellinus said "But that one judge was forced to take his seat on the skin of another who had been condemned to death for injustice" and that some laws "provide that because of the guilt of a single person all his relatives are put to death."[1,010]

Human sacrifice

♠ Prisoners ♣ absent ♥ There was animal sacrifice in Zoroastrianism but human sacrifice "was certainly profoundly at odds with Zoroastrian morality".[1,011] "Among faults committed by priests was the failure to recite the Gathas, which could be atoned for with an excellent draft animal or a team of two draft animals ... The fifth time, however, it became an ardush sin, and one's body was forfeit."[1,012] in what way was one's body forfeit? does this mean the death penalty or some other form of punishment? They flay men alive, either bit by bit or all at once, and no servant who waits upon them, or stands at table, is allowed to open his mouth, either to speak or to spit; to such a degree, after the skins are spread, are the mouths of all fettered."[1,013] Note that Ammianus Marcellinus is only one source and a Roman one at that so perhaps there could be political bias here - reporting one case as if it was a general feature of the Sasanid elites. however, an {absent; present} code could be appropriate the Phase II human sacrifice variable - at least for early Sasanid history. did the early Sasanid kings "claim the power of life and death over slaves and commons" because they considered themselves "from the lineage of the gods", "of the race of the gods", the "earthly incarnation of the supreme deity"? The idea among the Sasanid elite must have been powerful because it was around even in the early seventh century when Queen Boran attempted, against the ideology of the Zoroastrian authorities, to be the "restorer of her lineage, the race of gods". in coding the variable the key point for us is that at least by the time of Ammainus Marcellinus the ritual, if it was part of one, was kept quiet. that means it was not part of the official state ideology and could not impact society to, for example, display the power of the king. the early kingship, however, was more powerful so perhaps we should code that unknown.This is a question for an expert.

Religion and Normative Ideology[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni; Edward A L Turner ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Description of the Normative Ideology

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ present ♥ "The Sasanians identified Iran as the place where the ‘good religion’ (MP weh den) predominated. As seen on Ardashir’s investiture relief above, for Sasanian monarchs it was all about the xwarrah, the divine fortune or glory, which was brought to the king, and then to the land and its inhabitants through the bestowal of power by Ohrmazd. [...] The image of a ram appears on Sasanian stucco work and personal seals as a symbol of the xwarrah. The xwarrah is also indicated in various motifs on the crowns of Sasanian monarchs, such as a bird with a pearl in its beak. The significance of the diadem of rule as the repository of xwarrah is evidenced on the Sasanian inscription of Narseh (293-302 CE) at Paikuli, which tells of the punishment of one Wahnam, who had been ‘driven by Ahriman and the devs’ to place the crown on the head of a false ruler. Wahnam’s punishment was to be bound and brought to Narseh on a maimed donkey, and then killed." [1,026]

"Early Sasanian art is of the proclamatory type. Designed to assert the divine nature of kingship and to reinforce the state religion, Zoroastrianism".[1,027]

The King of Kings was the "representative of Ohrmazd on earth and the enemy of the followers of Ahriman."[1,028]

Sasanian iconography and inscriptions say the rulers are blessed by the gods [1,029], but have not descended from them.

♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ inferred absent ♥ Based on Rose's description [1,030], the "supernatural energy" associated with rulers is closer to a blessing than a power that the rulers can control.

♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ absent ♥

300 CE

Early Sasanids in their imperial ideology "considered themselves from the lineage of the gods" and used the Achaemenid title "King of Kings."[1,031] However, the Kayanid (Avestan) ideology was used from the fourth to sixth centurey CE.[1,032]
"In official inscriptions the Sasanian kings called themselves 'Mazda - worshipping majesty, of the race of the gods'. According to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.5), the Sasanian king considered himself 'brother of the sun and moon. On reliefs, 'in the language of transparent symbols, the King of Kings is shown as the earthly incarnation of the supreme deity.'"[1,033]
The King of Kings was the "representative of Ohrmazd on earth and the enemy of the followers of Ahriman."[1,034]
there might have been arbitrary punishments or sacrifices that were not part of the official doctrine, that was kept quiet: according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) the Sassanid elite claimed "the power of life and death over slaves and commons. They flay men alive, either bit by bit or all at once, and no servant who waits upon them, or stands at table, is allowed to open his mouth, either to speak or to spit; to such a degree, after the skins are spread, are the mouths of all fettered."[1,035]
Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[1,036]

400 CE

"By the end of the fourth century, the sacral aspect of kingship was curtailed by the increasingly powerful Zoroastrian clergy, and from the sixth century onwards it gravitated toward the title and conception of xwarrah (glory) of the King of Kings of Iranians."[1,037]
"The Sasanians also emphasized the millenarian aspect of Zoroastrianism, which waited for the savior to usher in the end of the world and of time."[1,038]

625 CE

Early seventh century, Queen Boran minted ceremonial gold coins "that stated she was the restorer of her lineage, the race of gods, which was emphasized in the early Sasanian period."[1,039]

Note that Ammianus Marcellinus is only one source and a Roman one at that so perhaps there could be political bias here - reporting one case as if it was a general feature of the Sasanid elites. however, an {absent; present} code could be appropriate the Phase II human sacrifice variable - at least for early Sasanid history. did the early Sasanid kings "claim the power of life and death over slaves and commons" because they considered themselves "from the lineage of the gods" , "of the race of the gods", the "earthly incarnation of the supreme deity"? The idea among the Sasanid elite must have been powerful because it was around even in the early seventh century when Queen Boran attempted, against the ideology of the Zoroastrian authorities, to be the "restorer of her lineage, the race of gods".

♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ absent ♥ "Shapur mentions in his inscription that many Wahram fires (meaning victorious fires) were established and that lamb, wine, and bread were offered to the gods for the souls of the kings and queens of the family of Sasan."[1,040] Though the privileged relationship between rulers and gods is a common subject in Sasanian iconography and inscriptions [1,041], nothing in the sources indicates that rulers became gods after death.

♠ Automatic deification ♣ inferred absent ♥ Though the privileged relationship between rulers and gods is a common subject in Sasanian iconography and inscriptions [1,042], nothing in the sources indicates that rulers became gods after death.

♠ Rulers are gods ♣ absent ♥

300 CE

"In official inscriptions the Sasanian kings called themselves 'Mazda - worshipping majesty, of the race of the gods'. According to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.5), the Sasanian king considered himself 'brother of the sun and moon. On reliefs, 'in the language of transparent symbols, the King of Kings is shown as the earthly incarnation of the supreme deity.'"[1,045]
"Early Sasanian art is of the proclamatory type. Designed to assert the divine nature of kingship and to reinforce the state religion, Zoroastrianism".[1,046]
The King of Kings was the "representative of Ohrmazd on earth and the enemy of the followers of Ahriman."[1,047]

400 CE

"By the end of the fourth century, the sacral aspect of kingship was curtailed by the increasingly powerful Zoroastrian clergy, and from the sixth century onwards it gravitated toward the title and conception of xwarrah (glory) of the King of Kings of Iranians."[1,049]
"The Sasanians also emphasized the millenarian aspect of Zoroastrianism, which waited for the savior to usher in the end of the world and of time."[1,050]

625 CE

Early seventh century, Queen Boran minted ceremonial gold coins "that stated she was the restorer of her lineage, the race of gods, which was emphasized in the early Sasanian period."[1,051]

Note that Ammianus Marcellinus is only one source and a Roman one at that so perhaps there could be political bias here - reporting one case as if it was a general feature of the Sasanid elites. however, an {absent; present} code could be appropriate the Phase II human sacrifice variable - at least for early Sasanid history. did the early Sasanid kings "claim the power of life and death over slaves and commons" because they considered themselves "from the lineage of the gods" , "of the race of the gods", the "earthly incarnation of the supreme deity"? The idea among the Sasanid elite must have been powerful because it was around even in the early seventh century when Queen Boran attempted, against the ideology of the Zoroastrian authorities, to be the "restorer of her lineage, the race of gods".


Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

(CC: descriptions provided of Zoroastrianism suggest that equality is ideologically reinforced. I have changed the codes to reflect this. However, descriptions also suggest that women were treated as property, so I've changed the codes to reflect sexual inequality)

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ present ♥ Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [1,052]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [1,053]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [1,054], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners.

♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ present ♥ Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [1,055]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [1,056]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [1,057], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners.
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ present ♥ Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [1,058]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [1,059]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [1,060], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners.
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ absent ♥ There is disagreement about the degree of sexual equality in Zoroastrianism. According to more ancient Indo-Iranian religious traditions, women could not hope for 'ascent to paradise' but were consigned, along with slaves, to 'the kingdom of shadows beneath the earth'.[1,061] Ketayun Gould thinks that Zoroastrian teachings then 'challenged the status quo by introducing a more gender-inclusive philosophy'. 'Paradise was no longer the exclusive domain of men who had the means to achieve it'.[1,062] Similarly, 'Mary Boyce and Albert de Jong argued that women were generally held in high esteem and occupied a dignified position in Zoroastrianism, their negative portrayal within the same tradition being only isolated and occasional occurrences and in any case inconsistent with Zoroastrian teaching'.[1,063] However, Jamsheed Choksy's book 'Evil, Good, and Gender' challenged this widespread opinion, pulling together malign images of women and femininity (e.g. female demons) from a variety of Zoroastrian texts and arguing that these symbolic structures would have had direct implications for women's daily lives.[1,064] Rose describes how Zoroastrian concerns with ritual purity made 'all women of childbearing age' (along with male priests and male corpse-bearers) into 'liminal figures'; their 'lives have also been constantly circumscribed due to their biological production of "dead matter" through menstruation or childbirth'.[1,065] The position of women in this religious tradition remains 'paradoxical' because 'In the past, the notion of gender equality in terms of the spiritual potential of women has been tempered by the regular exclusion of women from the ritual domain, due to their particular physiology'.[1,066] Also, regarding the Sasanians specifically: "The decisions reflect the practice of allowing [upper-class] Sasanian women (as widows or heirs) to manage the family estate, a feature of Iranian life that was noted earlier in Achaemenid times, and that is also considered permissible in the Herbedestan. A daughter could also inherit the familyor hearth-fire from her father, but upon marriage would hand over responsibility for it to her husband." [1,067]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ inferred absent ♥ "The Sasanians identified Iran as the place where the ‘good religion’ (MP weh den) predominated." [1,068]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ Zoroastrian eschatology held that the potential for resurrection and salvation was universal [1,069]. However, in the fifth century, a new sect, Mazdakism, advocated "egalitarianism in terms of sharing wealth and property, including women" and briefly resulted in "a land redistribution that diminished the power of both the priestly and upper classes, and benefitted the lower classes in both Iran and the client states to the west" [1,070]. This, as well as the emphasis placed in both iconography and inscriptions on the exhalted status of rulers and priests [1,071], suggests that the Zoroastrianism practiced by the Sasanians drew a stark line between rulers and commoners.

♠ Ideological thought distinguishes people from different locations ♣ inferred present ♥ "The Sasanians identified Iran as the place where the ‘good religion’ (MP weh den) predominated." [1,082]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ inferred present ♥ A passage from Shaki's article suggests that in Zoroastrian thought, elites were understood as cosmologically distinct from other social groups: 'The omniscient Mazdean religion is likened to a mighty tree with one trunk (the mean), two main boughs (action and abstention), three branches (good thoughts, good words, and good deeds), four small branches (the estates of the priests, warriors, husbandmen, and artisans), five roots (the lord of the house, the village headman, the tribal chieftain, the ruler, and the highest religious authority, the representative of Zoroaster on earth ...), and above them all the head of all heads ... the king of kings, the ruler of the whole world.'[1,083] (CC: note contradiction with previous information provided for previous code.)

♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ inferred absent ♥ In Middle Persian Zoroastrian texts, polygamy appears an 'ancient privilege of the aristocracy and spiritual dignitaries'.[1,084] No information is available regarding commoners, though it's notable that polygamy is not among the crimes recognised by Sasanian law [1,085]

♠ Ideological thought reinforces prosociality ♣ present ♥ "Another important surviving book is a work of religious instruction addressed to the laity, entitled the ‘Judgments of the Spirit of Wisdom’ (Dadestan-i Menog-i Xrad: MX). This text refers to the moral principles that should direct the lay Zoroastrian’s life, including basic observances of ‘good work’, such as prayer, wearing the sudreh and kusti, venerating the yazatas, celebrating the gahanbars (seasonal festivals) and religious rites, but not venerating idols or devs, nor burying the dead. ‘Good deeds’ also include caring for the earth, water and fire, as well as helping the poor." [1,086] In Zoroastrian tradition after death bones (asti) and (breath-)soul (urwan) are 'wrenched apart'. The breath-soul is judged at the Ford of the Accountant "where rewards or punishments are meted out according to the total account of the persons good and evil thoughts, words, and deeds." The souls of the rightuous go to a good place - perhaps a House of Songs - while the wicked souls are taken to a bad place e.g. 'Endless Darkness'.[1,087]

♠ charity ♣ present ♥ "Another important surviving book is a work of religious instruction addressed to the laity, entitled the ‘Judgments of the Spirit of Wisdom’ (Dadestan-i Menog-i Xrad: MX). This text refers to the moral principles that should direct the lay Zoroastrian’s life, including basic observances of ‘good work’, such as prayer, wearing the sudreh and kusti, venerating the yazatas, celebrating the gahanbars (seasonal festivals) and religious rites, but not venerating idols or devs, nor burying the dead. ‘Good deeds’ also include caring for the earth, water and fire, as well as helping the poor." [1,088]
♠ production of public goods ♣ inferred present ♥ Balali, Keulartz and Korthals link Zoroastrian ethics to the construction of qanats, an ancient irrigation system, in pre-Islamic Iran. Through the Zoroastrian exhortation to carry out 'good deeds', they argue that the worshipper 'is directed to relieve the poor, to irrigate and cultivate the soil, to provide food and fresh water in places where needed, and to devote the surplus of his wealth in charity to the well-being and prosperity of his fellow man'.[1,089] However, the focus of their article is on the lessons we could learn from 'traditional' systems of water management, rather than on rigorous historical analysis. It is unclear to what extent worshippers of Ahura Mazda in this specific polity actually laboured to produce public goods, directed solely by their religious values.
♠ communal dining ♣ present ♥ Evidence from rock reliefs and silver dishes show the "Persian concept of a royal banquet ... included wine drinking, feasting, music, and games being played before the king and the courtiers".[1,090] According to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) "All of them without exception, even at banquets and on festal days, appear girt with swords; an old Greek custom".[1,091]
♠ morality ♣ present ♥ "Another important surviving book is a work of religious instruction addressed to the laity, entitled the ‘Judgments of the Spirit of Wisdom’ (Dadestan-i Menog-i Xrad: MX). This text refers to the moral principles that should direct the lay Zoroastrian’s life, including basic observances of ‘good work’, such as prayer, wearing the sudreh and kusti, venerating the yazatas, celebrating the gahanbars (seasonal festivals) and religious rites, but not venerating idols or devs, nor burying the dead. ‘Good deeds’ also include caring for the earth, water and fire, as well as helping the poor." Normative Ideological Aspects of Slavery[edit]

♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-ethnics ♣ present ♥ "Slavery was hereditary (Madayan, pt. 1, pp. 1, 96), and, according to the jurist Syawaxs, against Rad-Hormizd, no Persian subject (bandag i sahansah) could be enslaved (Madayan, pt. 1, p. 20)."[1,095]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery for co-religionists ♣ present ♥ "If a Christian slave embraced Zoroastrianism and took to the people of the good religion (hu-dēnān) it was incumbent upon them to buy him out of slaver; but if he did not take to the Zoroastrians, he could procure his own manumission (Mādayān, pt. 1, p. 1)." [1,096]
♠ Doctrine forbids slavery universally ♣ absent ♥ "The institution of slavery (see BARDA AND BARDADĀRĪ) seems to have been more highly developed in the Sasanian period than previously, as is confirmed by the quantity of legislation relating to it in Middle Persian literature." [1,097]

Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)

♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic ♥ "Zarathustra had a particularly close relationship with Ahura Mazdā, from whom he received revelatory visions (daēnā-). His vision, expressed in the Gathas, included a radical transformation of traditional beliefs. In place of the pantheon he elevated Ahura Mazdā to a position of supremacy that approaches monotheism and surrounded him with a group of abstract entities, the Aməša Spəntas, all of whom perpetuate key concepts of Iranian religion as hypostases of Ahura Mazdā."[1,111]
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ present ♥ "The name of the supreme God of the Zoroastrian faith is Ahura Mazda (or Ohrmazd). He is self-created, omniscient, omnipresent, holy, invisible, and beyond human conceptualization (Yasna 4.7, 29.4, 31.13, 45.5; Yasht 1.7, 1.8, 1.12, 1.13-14; Videvdat 2.1, 19.20)."[1,112]
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ inferred present ♥ 'Ahura-Mazda' originally meant 'the all-knowing Lord', but by Achaemenid times had become simply a name.[1,113] However, the quality of divine omniscience seems to have been retained: Whitley uses Yasna 29 to assert that in Zoroastrianism, 'the universe, man and mind are alike created and sustained by Ahura Mazda, omniscient of past, present and future'.[1,114] Shaki also refers to the 'omniscient Mazdean religion'.[1,115]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ absent ♥ "In taking the immense step of seeing Ahura Mazdā as God, rather than only as one of the great gods of the Iranian pantheon, Zoroaster stopped short of conceiving him as omnipotent. According to his revelation, suffering and evil in this world, and torment in the next, have a source other than Ahura Mazdā. He can diminish and in the end annihilate them, but not control or direct them in the present."[1,116]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ moralizing ♥ The following quote makes it clear the Zoroastrian god, Ahura Mazda, was both responsible for the creation of the world and for punishing transgressors. "[T]he prophet feels also the awe due to the God who is “Creator of all” (Y. 44.7), and who, as Lord of aša, presides over a strict justice for mankind by which the wicked, who have chosen to act “at Lie’s commands” (Y. 31.1) will be in due course condemned. Their destination is the Worst Existence, i.e., Hell, which has been brought into being by Angra Mainyu (Y. 30.4). Mazdā has “appointed recompenses” (Y. 43.5), decreeing that “the end shall be different for each” (Y. 48.4); but he “has left to men’s wills (the choice between) holy and unholy” (Y. 45.9), and it is their own inner selves (daēnā-) which lead sinners to destruction (Y. 51.13)."[1,117]

Supernatural Enforcement of Morality

For this section, include any type of supernatural punishment or reward either in this lifetime (e.g., curses, good fortune) or in the afterlife (e.g., hell, reincarnation). This includes generalized impersonal punishment/reward (e.g., karma, mana) as well as punishment by specific supernatural beings. Include specific details of the nature and extent of punishment/reward in the description. Code “compound” moral actions (e.g., filial piety = kin + respect) in multiple categories as necessary. Morality categories are based on Moral Foundations Theory (Haidt & Graham, 2007) and Morality-As-Cooperation theory (Curry, 2016).
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ present ♥ "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Ardā Virāz Nāmag (The Book of the Righteous Virāz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[1,118] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include homicide, "neglecting crying and hungry children" and "denying hospitality to travellers"[1,119].
♠ supernatural enforcement of fairness ♣ present ♥ "The easiest way to avoid hell, of course, is to accumulate more good thoughts, words or deeds than bad. Some virtues, however, are praised as particularly efficient to avoid hell.20 A catechism highlights gratefulness (ČHP 30 [= Kanga 1960:16-17]). This virtue is also praised in the wisdom literature as a way to save one’s soul (Dk. VI 120; E38c; E45f [= Shaked 1979:48-49; 206-7; 214-15]), sometimes in conjunction with other virtues such as contentment and tenderness. Generosity is also emphasized as saving the soul from hell (Dk. IX 6.3 [see West 1892:179]), as is righteousness (Dk. IX 17.3 [see West 1892:204])."[1,120]
♠ supernatural enforcement of human reciprocity ♣ present ♥ "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Ardā Virāz Nāmag (The Book of the Righteous Virāz). No less than 84 of the 101 chapters (according to the standard modern editions) of this text deal with hell; it is the most detailed description of the other world available in Zoroastrian literature. Given its textual history and various translations, it is also one of the most popular religious writings of the Zoroastrians. The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[1,121] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "violation of contracts", "selling items with false measures and weights" and "making false promises"[1,122].
♠ supernatural enforcement of property rights ♣ present ♥ "Be that as it may, another division of hell appears in the Ardā Virāz Nāmag (The Book of the Righteous Virāz). The work is impossible to date with any amount of accuracy."[1,123] According to the Arda Viraz Namag, sins punished in hell include "acquisition of wealth by stealing the property of others"[1,124].
