♠ other supernatural enforcement of piety♣ present ♥ "Likewise there are some sins that immediately lead to hell, such as performing worship while thinking that the gods do not exist (Dk. VI D1b [= Shaked 1979:176-77]), standing when urinating (MX 1.39 [Skjærvø 2005:242]), or ignorance, bad knowledge and lack of wisdom (WZ 30.38-39 [= Gignoux and Tafazzoli 1993:104-7])."[1,134]
♠ other supernatural enforcement not related to piety ♣ present ♥ "Likewise there are some sins that immediately lead to hell, such as performing worship while thinking that the gods do not exist (Dk. VI D1b [= Shaked 1979:176-77]), standing when urinating (MX 1.39 [Skjærvø 2005:242]), or ignorance, bad knowledge and lack of wisdom (WZ 30.38-39 [= Gignoux and Tafazzoli 1993:104-7])."[1,135]

Human Enforcement of Morality

♠ care/harm ♣ inferred absent ♥ The following quote suggests that the Sasanians practiced human enforcement of morality, but not specifically relating to this variable, which seems more closely related to "sin regarding adversaries", punished by courts. "The term wināh ī ruwānīg was used of offenses endangering the soul of the delinquent, committed against religious norms, such as the obligation to avoid harming beneficent animals willfully or unwillfully and the duty to feed and protect animals in one’s guardianship. Wināh ī hamēmālān “sin regarding adversaries,” on the other hand, was used with regard to offenses which could be taken to court. It must have originally comprised both civil and criminal offenses, since it is used of all types of offenses leading to clashes between men. These could be controversies regarding the right to a certain property or loans and debts (which would belong to civil law nowadays) as well as complaints relating to criminal acts, such as theft, robbery, assault, manslaughter, murder (which belong to criminal law today)."[1,136]
♠ fairness/cheating ♣ inferred absent ♥ The following quote suggests that the Sasanians practiced human enforcement of morality, but not specifically relating to this variable, which seems more closely related to "sin regarding adversaries", punished by courts. "The term wināh ī ruwānīg was used of offenses endangering the soul of the delinquent, committed against religious norms, such as the obligation to avoid harming beneficent animals willfully or unwillfully and the duty to feed and protect animals in one’s guardianship. Wināh ī hamēmālān “sin regarding adversaries,” on the other hand, was used with regard to offenses which could be taken to court. It must have originally comprised both civil and criminal offenses, since it is used of all types of offenses leading to clashes between men. These could be controversies regarding the right to a certain property or loans and debts (which would belong to civil law nowadays) as well as complaints relating to criminal acts, such as theft, robbery, assault, manslaughter, murder (which belong to criminal law today)."[1,137]
♠ in-group loyalty ♣ present ♥ "The description of the real penalties which Sasanian law applied to Christian converts who apostasized from Zoroastrianism may be taken into account here. [...] In the Coptic Homilies too is described how those who left the Zoroastrian religion for Christianity lost their possessions by expropriation. Their worldly goods were plundered and they were banished from their homes. Those who had held titles or offices were dismissed. But in most cases mentioned the Christian martyrs were fettered with bonds of many kinds on their hands and feet and had to wear heavy chains of iron and collars round their necks. They were held in bondage and had to suffer hunger and thirst, cold, heat and darkness; they were sentenced to prison under the worst conditions; some of them were even walled up. They were beaten and scourged with whips of leather; they were skinned or combed with iron combs to tear their flesh to pieces. Very often they were sentenced to mutilation by the cutting-off of limbs (hands, feet, arms, legs), tongue, the ears and nose together, or the scalp. They also suffered blinding by means of knives or hot iron nails and the cauterizing of their mouths, ears and eyes with vinegar and mustard. Most of these torments were carried out immediately before the actual execution, or they led to death directly."[1,138]
♠ respect for authority ♣ present ♥ "Middle Persian inscriptions of Sasanian kings and those of one politically-powerful priest present a perspective on the Zoroastrian religion that takes little account of normative lay practice, but emphasizes the role of priests and monarchs as the key purveyors of religion." [1,139] In Zoroastrian tradition there are 'evil people'. The list includes people who break the law.[1,140] "The ultimate aim of education was ethical, e.g., 'good conduct,' a primary principle of Zoroastrianism (Arjmand 2008, 119-120)."[1,141]
♠ purity ♣ present ♥ The following quote clearly shows that purity was important for Zoroastrians, but it's not clear that it was rewarded or its opposite punished. "The purity regulations in the central section of the Videvdad reflect the understanding of a material division between ‘good’ and ‘evil’. Discussions about how to dispose of ‘dead matter’ (nasu) so as not to pollute the elements of the world, particularly water, fire or the ashavan, hinge on the distinction between that which brings growth and increase, and that which brings decay. In Videvdad, nasu has become hypostatized as a female demon, [Druj ] Nasush, who pollutes the human body, so that it constantly produces dead matter in the form of effluent such as saliva, mucus and women’s menses. Cuttings of hair and fingernails are also considered as dead matter (Vd 17), and the body of a corpse is the most polluting of all. The ashavan is encouraged always to be on the alert not to let such dead matter near the good creations, and this is why one should not urinate or spit into streams. A female in menses is to isolate herself in a place removed from people, fire and water (Vd 16). Pollution by such dead matter can be removed by cleansing first with bull’s urine and then water (Vd 19.- 5). The wearing of the kusti is advocated for men and women over 15 years of age as a barrier against the onslaught of evil at both material and conceptual levels (Vd 18.54- 9)." [1,142]
♠ kinship ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The following quote suggests that the Sasanians practiced human enforcement of morality, but it's not clear that transgressions against kinship were transgressions against religious norms. "The term wināh ī ruwānīg was used of offenses endangering the soul of the delinquent, committed against religious norms, such as the obligation to avoid harming beneficent animals willfully or unwillfully and the duty to feed and protect animals in one’s guardianship. Wināh ī hamēmālān “sin regarding adversaries,” on the other hand, was used with regard to offenses which could be taken to court. It must have originally comprised both civil and criminal offenses, since it is used of all types of offenses leading to clashes between men. These could be controversies regarding the right to a certain property or loans and debts (which would belong to civil law nowadays) as well as complaints relating to criminal acts, such as theft, robbery, assault, manslaughter, murder (which belong to criminal law today)."[1,143]
♠ bravery ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Moralistic punishment by human religious agents ♣ present ♥ "Being based on religion, Zoroastrian law only differentiated between “sins (or offenses) regarding the soul” (winah i ruwanig) and “offenses (or sins) regarding adverseries” (winah i hamemalan) (Macuch, 2003[a], p. 172 ff.). The former category comprised all offenses against religious norms which could endanger the soul of the delinquent (a few examples are given above) and were subject to punishment by religious authorities." [1,145]

Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):

♠ physical punishment ♣ present ♥ "The description of the real penalties which Sasanian law applied to Christian converts who apostasized from Zoroastrianism may be taken into account here. [...] In the Coptic Homilies too is described how those who left the Zoroastrian religion for Christianity lost their possessions by expropriation. Their worldly goods were plundered and they were banished from their homes. Those who had held titles or offices were dismissed. But in most cases mentioned the Christian martyrs were fettered with bonds of many kinds on their hands and feet and had to wear heavy chains of iron and collars round their necks. They were held in bondage and had to suffer hunger and thirst, cold, heat and darkness; they were sentenced to prison under the worst conditions; some of them were even walled up. They were beaten and scourged with whips of leather; they were skinned or combed with iron combs to tear their flesh to pieces. Very often they were sentenced to mutilation by the cutting-off of limbs (hands, feet, arms, legs), tongue, the ears and nose together, or the scalp. They also suffered blinding by means of knives or hot iron nails and the cauterizing of their mouths, ears and eyes with vinegar and mustard. Most of these torments were carried out immediately before the actual execution, or they led to death directly."[1,146] "Furthermore, there is plenty of evidence that, besides fines, in criminal cases also corporal punishment was executed. In the context of theft (duzīh) a punishment called drōš (< Av. draoša- “mutilation, maiming, brand”), was applied, which probably consisted of branding the face or cutting off a member (e.g., the nose), hereby stigmatizing the delinquent visibly (see references, Macuch, 1993, p. 702; compare Boyce, 1968, pp. 42 f.)." [1,147]

Extent of punishment (can be more than one):

♠ punishment of rulers ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the fact that sources don't mention instances of rulers being punished, as well as the fact that, when high priest Kawad I persuaded the king to enact land reforms based on heterodoxical beliefs, the priest was punished, not the king [1,151].
♠ punishment of elites ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the fact that a high-ranking priest, Kawad I, was imprisoned for promoting heterodoxical beliefs [1,152].
♠ punishment of commoners ♣ inferred present ♥ It seems reasonable to infer that Sasanian laws mostly applied to commoners.

Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ [absent; present] ♥ "Banking was well advanced."[1,153] The word "cheque" has a Persian root.[1,154] Sassanid kings took loans from banks.[1,155] "During the 3rd century A.D. banks in Persia and other territories in the Persian Empire under the Sassanid Empire issued letters of credit known as 'Sakks'."[1,156] "Banking in Iran dates back to 600 BCE ... Private banks, such as those of the Egibi family and the Murashu family, were founded at that time and engaged in mortgages, deposits, and loans. Customers possessed checkbooks and invested in property, farms, slaves, cattle, irrigation projects, fishing, and commercial ships. The Egibi family had the oldest banking institution in Persia, with headquarters in Ecbatana, Pasargadae, Babylonia, Jerusalem, and Sardis at the time of Cyrus the Great. The bank was responsible for collecting taxes and fees for the central government."[1,157] Zoroastrian priests surely would have had something to say about finance In Zoroastrian tradition Mithra was the guardian of contracts. These came in six different types of ascending merit: oral; handshake; promise of a sheep; promise of a horse/bull; promise of a man; promise of land.[1,158]
♠ Price controls ♣ ♥
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ ♥
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ [absent; present] ♥ Zoroastrian priests surely would have had something to say about finance In Zoroastrian tradition Mithra was the guardian of contracts. These came in six different types of ascending merit: oral; handshake; promise of a sheep; promise of a horse/bull; promise of a man; promise of land.[1,159]
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ inferred present ♥ Property could be "dedicated to the salvation of a man's soul" which could be used for ritual purposes after death, to build public works, establish pious foundations and fire-temples. Profit from pious foundations could be spent for a variety of purposes including alms to the poor.[1,160] "the major centralized forms of charity came from Zoroastrian institutions".[1,161] Under the influence of Zoroastrian priest Mazdak, Kavad I (488-496 CE and 498-531 CE) attempts dramatic reforms (giving away imperial granaries and redistributing land to peasants). A "time of chaos" ensued. Kavad was arrested and imprisoned, when he subsequently escaped to exile.[1,162] Christian xenodocheia also existed in Sasanid domains: "Christian charitable institutions - such as xenodocheia and nosokomeia - in the Byzantine Levant and Asia Minor, beginning in the late fourth century and progressing into the seventh. ... There is little evidence to suggest that these institutions were highly medicalized or were exclusively concerned with sick people. Instead, they appear to have been focused on the service of the poor and needy, even though any were more populated with the sick, the crippled, or the leper."[1,163]
♠ Pensions ♣ ♥
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ present ♥ Standardization in the region since the Achaemenids.[1,164]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ present ♥ "A constant denomination and weight standard were adopted".[1,165] "Striking coins was always a royal prerogative, and during the entire Sasanian history the typology employed is the same over the entire empire, proving that the mints always were under control of the royal central authorities."[1,166] "The Sasanians adopted the traditional silver drachm of Attic weight, the most common currency of the Parthian period. The weight (almost 4g) and the fineness of the metal used were, with few exceptions), well maintained."[1,167]
♠ Public markets ♣ present ♥ Sasanid administration employed a market inspector called a wazarbed.[1,168]

Contracts

♠ State enforcement ♣ present ♥ "Doctors possessed a type of licence authorizing them to engage in medical practice."[1,169] Doctors required a licence and the medical community had a hierarchy. There was a distinction between doctors who were concerned with the body and those who worked on the spirit. The later were considered part of the priest caste, and at the top the mobadan mobad ("state's leading religious dignitary") was above the drustbadh ("state's chief doctor").[1,170] if doctors tried to practice without a licence what would happen? what would happen to bad doctors? the state must have enforced this, with the state's chief doctor having ultimate responsibility.
♠ Non-state enforcement ♣ present ♥ In Zoroastrian tradition Mithra was the guardian of contracts. These came in six different types of ascending merit: oral; handshake; promise of a sheep; promise of a horse/bull; promise of a man; promise of land.[1,171] From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline." [1,172] religious hierarchy and the state were closely entwined so we should code enforcement for both state and non-state (religious) enforcement.
♠ Corporate Agency ♣ present ♥ From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline." [1,173] religious communities were organized as legal corporations and held a responsibility to the state for services required to undertake: taxes and discipline. this reads like state contracting with a corporate agency.
♠ State contracting ♣ present ♥ From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline." [1,174] religious communities were organized as legal corporations and held a responsibility to the state for services required to undertake: taxes and discipline. this reads like state contracting with a corporate agency.

♠ Drinking water ♣ present ♥ "In the year 326 AD when the city of Susa was destroyed during an earthquake, Shapur ordered it to be rebuilt with all the urban facilities, including water flowing in every house, a sewer system and a laundry in each neighbourhood (Hashami, 2010)."[1,183]

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ ♥

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ "From the earliest times [in Babylonia], the flow of water was controlled for agricultural purposes by an elaborate system of canals, sluices, dams, embankments, and dikes."[1,184] "It appears to have been during his reign that the irrigation works were constructed on the banks of the Karun river near Shushtar; the most famous is the Valerian dam and bridge. Faced with stone slabs, this enabled the water level in one of the Karun’s tributaries to be raised by 2 or 3 m. The length of the stone bridge, which is reminiscent of similar Roman structures, was over 500 m. Another bridge more than 400m long has been preserved in Dezful, not far from Susa. There were several bridges across ravines and rivers on the ancient road that linked Ecbatana (modern Hamadan) and Susa."[1,185]

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ inferred present ♥ Zoroastrian festivals in ancient Iran included Mehragan, Tiragan and Nowruz. Nowruz was one of the seven annual feasts with mandatory participation.[1,187]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ ♥

♠ Theatres ♣ inferred absent ♥ Invernizzi "noted that Tell Umar 'conceals the remains of a large theatre ... In late Sassanid times the remains of the theatre were included within a powerful tower of uncertain purpose.'"[1,188]" Cannot find any references to theatres as permanent specialist structures. Only reference was this one to the ruins of a Greek theatre at Seleucia-on-the-Tigris.
♠ Arenas ♣ inferred absent ♥ Land owning nobility played polo and tennis, and enjoyed music and singing. This infers specialised architectural structures such as fields, courts and stages and places for viewing. "Their lives were filled with martial training, hunting, feasting, the enjoyment of women, literature, chess, tennis, polo, music, singing and graphic art. The descriptions of early travellers and historians, and the evidence of archaeology, agree in ascribing to the Sassanid monarchy and nobility a dazzling richness of architectural decoration, costume, and jewelery." [1,189] Have not come across references to stadiums. These may have been last present during the Parthian era which still retained a large Greek community and culture.
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ "Three major fire-temples were established for the three classes ... Smaller fire-temples existed in the villages and towns".[1,190] Cube of Zoroaster was a sacred site for the Saravan.[1,191]

♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ present ♥ At Shapur I's palace at Bishapur ... "mosaics adorning the floor of the Grand Aiwan are in the Syro-Roman style - the dancing girls, musicians and theatrical masks depicted scenes from the Dionysiac cycle."[1,192] "There are many romantic stories about Bahram V, such as the importation of Indian minstrels as entertainers..."[1,193] Court life of Kushrau II shown by rock reliefs at Taq-e Bustan, "where the king is shown on a boat hunting and musicians playing their harps".[1,194]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ lower ♥ Hereditary elite were warriors and priests. They would not have considered themselves on a par with dancers and ministrels.

♠ Access to education ♣ present ♥ "Children of the nobility started school at the age of five to seven years and lasted until age fifteen. General education was composed of reading, writing, religious instruction, physical education, and courtly arts. Education included not only hunting and the arts of war but social manners as well."[1,195] Nestorian school at Nisibis "was a theological academy enjoying special privileges, produced a number of Nestorian scholars who made an important contribution to the history of ideas in the East."[1,196] Jews had their own law courts, schools and synagogues.[1,197] Scribal schools (dibirstan).[1,198] Scribal schools (dibirstan).[1,199] However: "In the strictly hierarchical administrative system in place during the Sassanid dynasty ... education was reserved for the elite."[1,200]

[1,203] Presumably funded by the court of the Sasanid king or rulers of provinces. Publically subsidized education not widespread as the government and religious hierarchy was dominated by an elite.
♠ Schools for children? ♣ present ♥ "Children of the nobility started school at the age of five to seven years and lasted until age fifteen. General education was composed of reading, writing, religious instruction, physical education, and courtly arts. Education included not only hunting and the arts of war but social manners as well."[1,204] "In the field of education, a short extant treatise entitled the Khweskarih-i Redagan [The Duties of Children] contains instructions concerning children's obligations towards their parents and teachers."[1,205] Jews had their own law courts, schools and synagogues.[1,206] Scribal schools (dibirstan).[1,207]
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ inferred present ♥ In addition to the Zoroastrian religious literature the Christian Syriac-Nestorian output also was considerable after c400 CE. "Its authors were mainly Iranians" and in Sasanian Iran there were "schools" in Nisibis (Mesopotamia) "founded after the closure in 489 of the Nestorian 'Persian school' in Edessa (founded in the fourth century by the Syrian, Ephraim), Ctesiphon and Gundeshapur ("the Syriac Bet Lapat") in Khuzistan.[1,208] Medical school of Gundi-Shapur founded by Shapur I (241-272 CE) in Susa and remained active at least until c800 CE when they provided the physicians at the court of the Abbasids.[1,209] Nestorian school at Gundeshapur had a medical academy attached to it founded by Shapur I (241-271 CE).[1,210] Nestorian school at Nisibis "was a theological academy enjoying special privileges, produced a number of Nestorian scholars who made an important contribution to the history of ideas in the East."[1,211] The Nestorian Christian academies appear to be places of higher learning.
♠ Libraries ♣ present ♥ "Important events of the reign of each of the Sasanian kings were written down and preserved in the imperial archives, a practice that probably dates from the very beginning of Sasanian rule."[1,212] Nestorian school at Nisibis "was a theological academy enjoying special privileges, produced a number of Nestorian scholars who made an important contribution to the history of ideas in the East."[1,213] "The city of Istakhr was known in the Sasanid period to have had a great repository of royal books."[1,214] The intellectual life within the Sasanid realm would not have been possible without libraries.
♠ Museums ♣ ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ [20-35] ♥ "Urban merchants were able to read, write, and count, but many peasants in rural areas were illiterate."[1,215] "Children of the nobility started school at the age of five to seven years and lasted until age fifteen. General education was composed of reading, writing, religious instruction, physical education, and courtly arts. Education included not only hunting and the arts of war but social manners as well."[1,216] Highly literate elite with literacy extending to the merchant class. The most literate region of the Sassanid Empire was probably Mesopotamia where there were was a history of literacy among Jewish and Christian religious communities (which had schools[1,217] and academies[1,218] for higher learning). In Mesopotamia and elsewhere in the Empire there was a thriving merchant life with advanced banking[1,219] and the bill of exchange"[1,220] attested. The Persians had a literate religious class of Zoroastrian priests while the administrative system was fed by recruits from scribal schools.[1,221] Of the four main classes within Sasanid society "warriors, scribes, priests, and commoners"[1,222] the commoners, who made up the bulk of the population, would be the least likely to have been literate (although they would include merchants). We might expect the warriors, who were an hereditary elite, to have had some literacy but not as much as the scribes and priests who were certainly literate. A figure of 20-35% may cover the likely range.

Biological Well-Being[edit]

Disease

♠ Endemic disease ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Disease outbreaks ♣ suspected unknown: 488 CE-542 CE; present: 543 CE-544 CE; suspected unknown: 545 CE-626 CE; present: 627 CE; suspected unknown: 628 CE-633 CE; present: 634 CE-642 CE ♥ "In 543 CE, plague reached what is now modern Iran after passing from Italy to Syria, Palestine, and Iraq, and infected the Persian imperial army and the population at that time [28]. In 544 CE, the plague infected the army of the Roman Empire and the army of the Persian Empire at a time when they were at war [29]. In 627 CE, a large epidemic of plague, which led to the death of more than 100,000 people in Ctesiphon, the Sassanian capital, close to Baghdad, was reported. Shortly thereafter, Shiroyeh, the king of Persia, died of plague [30]. Another plague epidemic occurred from 634 to 642 CE in the region of Yezdigird III, the ‘great king’ of Persia [31]. The Yezdigird Plague may be another name for the plague epidemic in Syria and Palestine that is known as the Amwas (or Amawas) Plague, which killed almost 25,000 people in 638 or 639 CE [32]. The Amwas Plague is considered one of the numerous outbreaks of plague in the sixth, seventh, and eighth centuries that followed the major pandemic of the sixth century, the Justinian Plague [33]."[1,223]

Prosocial Behavior[edit]

♠ Volunteering ♣ ♥

♠ Charity ♣ present ♥ Property could be "dedicated to the salvation of a man's soul" which could be used for ritual purposes after death, to build public works, establish pious foundations and fire-temples. Profit from pious foundations could be spent for a variety of purposes including alms to the poor.[1,224] "the major centralized forms of charity came from Zoroastrian institutions".[1,225] Zoroastrians founded pious foundations.[1,226] Christian xenodocheia also existed in Sasanid domains: "Christian charitable institutions - such as xenodocheia and nosokomeia - in the Byzantine Levant and Asia Minor, beginning in the late fourth century and progressing into the seventh. ... There is little evidence to suggest that these institutions were highly medicalized or were exclusively concerned with sick people. Instead, they appear to have been focused on the service of the poor and needy, even though any were more populated with the sick, the crippled, or the leper."[1,227] "The only connections between the Sassanid elites and the xendochoia arose through the Syriac physicians, who were becoming the most influential Syriac Christians in the Sassanid court. Sassanid patrons provided support to their physicians with the construction of the xendocheia, which had become deeply connected to the physicians' careers and lives."[1,228]

Voluntary Associations

♠ voluntary trade associations ♣ present ♥ Doctors required a licence and the medical community had a hierarchy. There was a distinction between doctors who were concerned with the body and those who worked on the spirit. The later were considered part of the priest caste, and at the top the mobadan mobad ("state's leading religious dignitary") was above the drustbadh ("state's chief doctor").[1,229]

♠ voluntary community organizations ♣ ♥

♠ prestige of voluntary athletic clubs organizations ♣ ♥

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ present ♥ In the Early Sasanian period vassals paid tribute.[1,230] "By the end of the fourth century, a system was beginning to form whereby the Christian bishop resided at the capital, Ctesiphon, along with the Jewish exilarch, the Resh Galut, and they paid taxes in return for peace and security."[1,231]

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ In rural areas wages and often taxes were paid in kind.[1,232]
♠ Labor ♣ ♥
♠ Currency ♣ inferred present ♥ "Under the Sasanids, the Silk Route flourished. The government controlled trade and imposed heavy taxes on all goods passing through its lands."[1,233] "Sasanid coins found in ports of southern China, and others discovered in northeastern China, indicate trade with Fars by the Silk Route by sea."[1,234] coins were used in international trade in this era so it is very likely taxes could be paid in currency.


Debt and Indemnities

♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ present ♥ Sassanids discontinue tribute payment to Byzantines? "The Byzantine chronicler Priscus mentions the 'Kidarite Huns' for the first time in 456 as adversaries of the Sasanian king Yazdgird II (438-457), who had discontinued a tribute paid by his predecessors to Iran’s eastern neighbours (Exc. De Leg. Rom. 8, ed. Blockley 1983, pp. 336-7)."[1,235] Tribute to the Hephthalites under Balash (484-488 CE).[1,236]
♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ present ♥ In the Early Sasanian period vassals paid tribute.[1,237]

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥ Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) ordered lands to be measured, fixed tax per griv (1/10 hectare) according to what was grown. Khusrau (later Sassanid period) demanded "all persons between the ages of 20 and 50, except nobles, soldiers and priests ... to pay the poll tax, whose amount ranged from 12 drachms (Arabic dirhams) to 4 drachms, according to wealth."[1,238] "Under the Sasanids, the Silk Route flourished. The government controlled trade and imposed heavy taxes on all goods passing through its lands."[1,239] Early seventh century, Queen Boran tried to "relieve the population of heavy taxes, as the Islamic sources report."[1,240]

"The seven families certainly exercised authority over entire provinces where the peasants were obliged to pay taxes to them in addition to the taxes demanded by the royal treasury."[1,241] Was this still true after the reforms of Khusrau I in the early sixth century?

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ At the time the Arabs took over in the seventh century they retained the Sasanid taxation system which included a land and poll tax.[1,242]
♠ Property ♣ ♥
♠ People ♣ inferred absent ♥ Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) demanded "all persons between the ages of 20 and 50, except nobles, soldiers and priests ... to pay the poll tax, whose amount ranged from 12 drachms (Arabic dirhams) to 4 drachms, according to wealth."[1,243] Khusrau I's reform of the tax system occurred in the sixth century so presumably there was no poll tax in this earlier period.
♠ Trade ♣ present ♥ "Under the Sasanids, the Silk Route flourished. The government controlled trade and imposed heavy taxes on all goods passing through its lands."[1,244]
♠ Income ♣ ♥
♠ Production ♣ present ♥ Arab historian al-Tabari claimed that "farmers had no right to harvest crops or gather fruit from their garden before the arrival of the tax collector; the long wait meant that their produce was frequently wasted."[1,245] In the sixth century this situation was reformed by Khusrau I's new fiscal system.[1,246]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ present ♥ "The great tax reform of Khusrau I marked a turning point in the Sasanian state administration. For the first time, the power of the landed nobility was restricted and all the taxes were in the hands of the king."[1,247] After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "Supervision of the payment of taxes into the royal treasury was undertaken by the hamarkar (accountant)".[1,248] After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "The taxes were collected according to the administrative sub-divisions of the country from village up to province, with the officer in charge of a province being responsible for overall supervision and the tax in each city being paid to the judge of that city."[1,249] Taxes were imposed by the king but it wasn't until Khusrau I's reforms in the sixth century that the system was centralized.
♠ local elites ♣ inferred present ♥ "The great tax reform of Khusrau I marked a turning point in the Sasanian state administration. For the first time, the power of the landed nobility was restricted and all the taxes were in the hands of the king."[1,250] After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "Supervision of the payment of taxes into the royal treasury was undertaken by the hamarkar (accountant)".[1,251] After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "The taxes were collected according to the administrative sub-divisions of the country from village up to province, with the officer in charge of a province being responsible for overall supervision and the tax in each city being paid to the judge of that city."[1,252] Khusrau I's reforms which centralized the tax system and put the collection of taxes in the hands of officials occurred in the sixth century. Before his time tax collection was therefore less centralized; apparently under the control of the landed nobility.
♠ state officials ♣ inferred absent ♥ "The great tax reform of Khusrau I marked a turning point in the Sasanian state administration. For the first time, the power of the landed nobility was restricted and all the taxes were in the hands of the king."[1,253] After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "Supervision of the payment of taxes into the royal treasury was undertaken by the hamarkar (accountant)".[1,254] After Khusrau I's (later Sassanid period) reform of the tax system "The taxes were collected according to the administrative sub-divisions of the country from village up to province, with the officer in charge of a province being responsible for overall supervision and the tax in each city being paid to the judge of that city."[1,255] Khusrau I's reforms which centralized the tax system and put the collection of taxes in the hands of officials occurred in the sixth century. Before his time tax collection was therefore less centralized; apparently under the control of the landed nobility.

Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ In rural areas wages and often taxes were paid in kind.[1,256]
♠ Labor ♣ ♥
♠ Currency ♣ inferred present ♥ "Under the Sasanids, the Silk Route flourished. The government controlled trade and imposed heavy taxes on all goods passing through its lands."[1,257] "Sasanid coins found in ports of southern China, and others discovered in northeastern China, indicate trade with Fars by the Silk Route by sea."[1,258] coins were used in international trade in this era so it is very likely taxes could be paid in currency.

HPI Variables[edit]

♠ Dedicated police force ♣ present ♥ Specialist law enforcement officials. Alexander the Great c330 BCE with the Macedonian conquest of Persia introduced the Greek Polis system which organized the law and order in the cities. "It is very likely that the term police in Western languages has its source in the Greek notion of Polis.” Under this system "An official named Epistat, appointed by the monarchy, oversaw the manner in which the Polis performed its duties. ... The Shahrik and the Dayhlik were two additional administrative and governing bodies whose development goes to the time of the Sassanid Empire (Kusha 2000). The Shahrik was responsible for law enforcement and security matters pertaining to cities, whereas the Dayhik performed these duties in villages. It is logical to assume that these order-imposing institutions borrowed legal and administrative concepts and technical know-how from their predecessors, for instance, from the Hellenistic institution of Polis mentioned above.”[1,260] From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline."</ref>(Lapidus 2012, 16) Lapidus, I M. 2012. Islamic Societies to the Nineteenth Century: A Global History. Cambridge University Press. Cambridge.</ref>
♠ Police levels ♣ 4 ♥ 1. King 2. Provincial governor 3. Sharik (city) or Dayhlik (village) 4. Policeman (in city)? Alexander the Great c330 BCE with the Macedonian conquest of Persia introduced the Greek Polis system which organized the law and order in the cities. "It is very likely that the term police in Western languages has its source in the Greek notion of Polis.” Under this system "An official named Epistat, appointed by the monarchy, oversaw the manner in which the Polis performed its duties. ... The Shahrik and the Dayhlik were two additional administrative and governing bodies whose development goes to the time of the Sassanid Empire (Kusha 2000). The Shahrik was responsible for law enforcement and security matters pertaining to cities, whereas the Dayhik performed these duties in villages. It is logical to assume that these order-imposing institutions borrowed legal and administrative concepts and technical know-how from their predecessors, for instance, from the Hellenistic institution of Polis mentioned above.”[1,261] From the 4th century: "religions communities were organized as legal corporations owning property; maintaining courts; regulating marriages, divorces and inheritances; and through their chiefs, holding responsibility to the state for taxes and discipline."</ref>(Lapidus 2012, 16) Lapidus, I M. 2012. Islamic Societies to the Nineteenth Century: A Global History. Cambridge University Press. Cambridge.</ref>
♠ Proscriptions of elites and or property ♣ ♥ Use of the state to repress or purge rival elite groups.
♠ Corporal punishment by the state ♣ ♥


♠ Military control over independent or semi-independent vassal states ♣ present ♥ Semi-independent vassal kingdoms (Merv, Kerman, Sakastan, Adiabene, Iberia, Makran, Mesene, Kushanshahr and Armenia).[1,262] "from the second quarter of the fifth century Kartli was a Sassanid vassal state, ruled by the Karlian king assisted by a pitiakhsh, a Persian deputy. ... Albania fell under the nominal domination of the Sassanids too, though it retained some autonomy, and its level of development was not as high as that of neigbouring Armenia or Kartli. The Sassanids were interested in Albania because it was the key to controlling the mountain passes of the main Caucasus range and therefore to repelling any attacks by the nomads of the Northern Caucasus (Alans, Huns, etc.)."[1,263]
